Preface
This thesis has been prepared over five and a half months, corresponding to 32.5 ECTS
credits, at the Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science at the TechôÙ
nical University of Denmark (DTU), in collaboration with Alfa Laval Copenhagen A/S. In
accordance with the confidentiality agreement with Alfa Laval, all proprietary data, examôÙ
ples, and figures presented in this thesis have been anonymized or generalized to protect
sensitive information. The work was undertaken in partial fulfillment of the requirements
for the MSc degree in HumanôÙCentered Artificial Intelligence.
Diogo Miguel Lopes Carvalho, s233176

Abstract
Industrial contract review presents a critical manual bottleneck, consuming significant exôÙ
pert time and introducing compliance risk. The ALDEVI system, an endôÙtoôÙend retrievalôÙ
augmented generation solution, was engineered to automate deviation detection. Its founôÙ
dation is the novel ãCompany Truthsã framework, structuring tacit expert knowledge and
formal standards to overcome the rigidity of traditional requirement templates. The opôÙ
timal system was selected from nine benchmarked architectures by fineôÙtuning text and
vision embeddings, optimizing prompt programs, and evaluating quantized models. It emôÙ
ploys a hybrid retrieval pipeline combining sparse, dense, and lateôÙinteraction methods.
On new projects, ALDEVI achieves an 88.4% recall rate and a 0.73 F1 score in deviation
detection, reducing manual review time from 30 to 8 hours per project while recovering
1,400 engineering hours annually. The system boosts throughput nearly fourfold at a cost
of under 5ã˜ per project.
Keywords:
Deviation Detectionëƒ Knowledge Representationëƒ Large Language Modelsëƒ Quantized
Modelsëƒ RetrievalôÙAugmented Generation

Introduction
1.1
The AI Imperative in Modern Engineering
For decades, Mooreãs Law set the pace of technological progress, with computing power
doubling roughly every two years. But in the era of Artificial Intelligence (AI), that steady
progression has given way to something much faster. According to research by METR, the
length of tasks AI systems can complete without human intervention is now doubling every
seven months [1]. Technologist David Giambruno has dubbed this trend Rayãs Law, the
idea that AI capabilities are improving at an unprecedented rate [2]. If this trend continues,
AI tools, especially generative AI, could become more than ten times more capable within
just two years, unlocking the ability to automate multiôÙstep tasks that currently take human
experts hours or even days to complete.
This is not just a theoretical projection but rather a commercial reality that is already reôÙ
shaping the way businesses operate. A recent McKinsey Global Survey found that 78%
of organizations are now using AI in at least one business function, a sharp rise from
55% just one year prior [3]. What was once considered experimental is now being inteôÙ
grated into core operations, with many companies reporting significant cost savings and
measurable revenue increases from their use of generative AI. The message is clear: AI
adoption is no longer optional, and for organizations that fail to adapt, the risk of being left
behind is both real and accelerating.
One area where the gap between exponential AI advancements and outdated manual
processes is particularly evident is in the technical due diligence performed on customer
tender documents. This largely manual review is essential for identifying and mitigating
contractual risks, yet its inefficiency creates significant business challenges. The time reôÙ
quired to review a single document manually varies significantly depending on its complexôÙ
ity and structure. Simple documents may take as little as 1ã2 hours, while more complex
(multiôÙdocument) agreements can require anywhere from 10 to 40 hours or more [4, 5].
Ultimately, these prolonged review cycles not only result in costly delays but also increase
the likelihood of human error, undermining the goal of ensuring contractual compliance
and negatively impacting financial performance.
These challenges are particularly evident at Alfa Laval, a global leader in industrial proôÙ
cess engineering, where the Engineering & Supply (E&S) department is responsible for
validating complex, highôÙstakes agreements against internal standards and practices.
This task is both timeôÙconsuming and errorôÙprone, relying heavily on expert judgment and
manual effort. Given the scale and importance of these agreements, the process serves
as a representative example of where AI could meaningfully enhance efficiency, accuôÙ
racy, and consistency. This thesis directly confronts this challenge within the highôÙstakes
industrial context of Alfa Laval, investigating how modern AI can be applied to solve a
concrete business problem and transform a critical operational workflow.
1.2
The Operational Bottleneck at Alfa Laval
This challenge is centered within the E&S department at Alfa Lavalãs Business Unit DeôÙ
canters. This team is responsible for a critical validation step: ensuring that every customôÙ
engineered solution is technically viable, compliant, and safe.
To do this, they must
conduct a thorough analysis of customer requirements against a complex set of interôÙ
nal practices, as well as international standards. From the moment a customer request is

received, this entire scope of work, encompassing all documentation, requirements, and
engineering analysis, is formally defined and managed as a project.
The core of the problem lies in the manual nature of this validation process. For each
project, E&S engineers must review dense customer tender documents, page by page,
and crossôÙreference them with information scattered across various internal systems. This
manual analysis is a primary source of inefficiency. For instance, a single project with
approximately 20 files can require over 30 hours of an engineerãs time. Critical knowledge
is not confined to a single, structured source but is instead fragmented across SharePoint
sites, historical project folders, and the individual expertise of team members. This manual
and disconnected workflow creates a significant operational bottleneck with several direct
consequences, as illustrated in Figure 1.1.

The primary outcomes of this inefficient process are:
ãÂ Increased Business Risk: Manual checks are prone to human error, creating a sigôÙ
nificant risk of missed deviations that can lead to costly rework, compliance failures,
and negative financial impacts.
ãÂ High Review Cycle Time: Significant time is lost searching for fragmented inforôÙ
mation and manually comparing documents, which directly increases project costs
and extends proposal timelines.
ãÂ Inconsistent Quality: Without a systematic process, the thoroughness of each
review relies on the individual engineer. Consequently, the application of company
standards becomes inconsistent, and quality assurance remains unpredictable.
ãÂ Lost Knowledge & Rework: Insights from past projects are isolated within static
documents, making them difficult to find and reuse. As a result, institutional knowlôÙ
edge is lost, and engineers are forced to ãreinvent the wheelã for recurring issues.
Together, these issues reveal a process under pressure. Operational inefficiency, compliôÙ
ance risk, and the ongoing loss of institutional knowledge are more than daily obstacles.
They pose a strategic threat.

1.3
Problem Statement
This thesis formalizes this strategic challenge into a central research question:
Primary Research Question
How can a RetrievalôÙAugmented Generation (RAG) system be engineered to transôÙ
form Alfa Lavalãs manual review of customer tender documents by accurately deôÙ
tecting deviations and increasing operational efficiency?
To provide a comprehensive answer, this research is structured around three key invesôÙ
tigative pillars, each formulated as a distinct subôÙquestion:
1. The Knowledge Foundation: How can unstructured company knowledge be efôÙ
fectively structured to serve as a reliable foundation for an AIôÙdriven system?
2. The System Optimization: Which RAG architecture and components provide the
most accurate and efficient performance for deviation detection in this specialized
industrial domain?
3. The Validation Framework: What is a practical methodology for validating the sysôÙ
temãs performance and quantifying its impact on the realôÙworld engineering workôÙ
flow?
Answering these questions requires a clear set of research goals and objectives, which
are detailed in the following section.
1.4
Goals and Objectives
To answer the research questions posed, the primary goal of the thesis was to design, deôÙ
velop, and validate a ProofôÙofôÙConcept (PoC) system. The resulting system, named Alfa
Laval Deviation Insight (ALDEVI), serves as the output of the research. Furthermore,
the project was structured around four distinct objectives, each forming a critical element
of the overall work.
1.4.1
Data Curation and Foundation
The first objective was to establish a highôÙquality dataset suitable for AI analysis. FounôÙ
dational work involved designing and implementing automated pipelines to ingest, clean,
and organize years of unstructured historical project data, creating a reliable basis for all
subsequent development.
1.4.2
Knowledge Representation
The second objective addressed the knowledge foundation question by developing a
novel framework for representing domainôÙspecific knowledge. A structured set of verifiôÙ
able statements was engineered from internal standards and historical findings, providing
a precise and reliable ãsource of truthã for the AI systemãs reasoning process.
1.4.3
System Design and Optimization
The third objective was to design and optimize the core AI engine systematically. In pursuit
of system optimization, a multiôÙstage investigation identified the optimal RAG architecture
through the benchmarking of various Large Language Model (LLM), prompt engineering
strategies, embedding models, and retrieval techniques, including fineôÙtuning and hybrid
search.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
3
==PAGE:18==
1.4.4
Prototype Validation and Impact Analysis
The final objective was to validate the complete ALDEVI prototype and quantify its realôÙ
world value. To answer the validation framework question, the userôÙfacing application was
tested by the E&S department experts on new projects. This final phase focused on meaôÙ
suring performance and gathering qualitative and quantitative feedback to demonstrate
the systemãs impact.
1.5
Scope and Limitations
To ensure a focused and achievable research project, several key boundaries were estabôÙ
lished. The research is based on a specific data scope from Alfa Lavalãs Business Unit
Decanters, encompassing two main categories of documents: historical project documenôÙ
tation, including customer tenders and previously identified deviations, and a collection
of internal reference materials, such as corporate standards and product manuals, which
serve as the baseline for compliance.
The task scope is primarily concerned with detecting deviations. In comparison, the
analysis of clarifications is regarded as an adjacent but distinct issue that lies outside
the core objectives of this work. A deviation represents a direct conflict with an internal
standard, whereas a clarification occurs when a customer requirement is ambiguous or
incomplete. For example, a request for ãa highôÙefficiency motorã without specifying a
standard, or a vague requirement to comply with ãall local regulations,ã illustrates such
ambiguity. Since resolving these points requires interactive negotiation with the customer
to determine their precise intent, they cannot be addressed through automated detection.
Therefore, the handling of clarifications falls beyond the scope of this project.
The projectãs technical scope was defined by a twoôÙphase approach dictated by availôÙ
able computational resources. Initial development and baseline model selection were
conducted on a resourceôÙconstrained local machine (MacBook Air M3), which necessiôÙ
tated the use of quantized models for feasible local inference. Subsequent work used a
multiôÙGPU environment, which significantly accelerated experimentation and enabled the
testing of more computationally intensive retrieval architectures and new modalities, such
as vision.
Finally, the output scope defines the deliverable as a functional PoC system designed
to demonstrate feasibility rather than a productionôÙready system fully integrated into the
Alfa Laval ecosystem.
1.6
Significance and Contribution
The significance of this research extends to both industrial practice at Alfa Laval and the
broader academic field of applied AI.
For Alfa Laval, the primary contribution is demonstrating a practical model for modernizing
core business processes with AI, thereby maintaining its competitive edge. This thesis
embodies a shift from traditional, centralized technology projects towards agile, Business
UnitôÙled initiatives where solutions are developed in direct collaboration with the domain
experts who will use them. The ALDEVI PoC was intentionally designed to augment,
not replace, the critical judgment of the E&S engineers. This expertôÙdriven approach
ensures the final tool is practical, trustworthy, and directly addresses the departmentãs
most pressing challenges. Its success provides a powerful blueprint for how the company
can responsibly and effectively explore and adopt advanced AI, building solutions that
empower its workforce and demonstrate a clear path for future integration with existing
enterprise systems.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG

==PAGE:19==
The academic contribution is threefold. First, it introduces a practical methodology for
turning ambiguous company documents and historical data into a structured knowledge
base. This approach establishes a foundation that is both verifiable by human experts
and readily usable by an AI system. Second, it provides dataôÙdriven findings from optiôÙ
mizing an entire RAG pipeline for a specialized technical domain. The research details
the performance tradeôÙoffs between key components, such as LLMs, chunking strateôÙ
gies, embedding models, and retrieval architectures, demonstrating the critical impact
of domainôÙspecific adaptations like fineôÙtuning. Ultimately, the thesis presents a compreôÙ
hensive, endôÙtoôÙend case study of engineering and validating an AI system in a realôÙworld
industrial setting, providing practical, inôÙtheôÙfield insights that complement theoretical reôÙ
search.
1.7
Thesis Outline
The outline for this thesis is as follows:
1. The first chapter ã Introduction ã introduces the operational challenges at Alfa
Laval and frames the research within the context of modern AI advancements. It
defines the core research questions, goals, scope, and contributions of the thesis.
2. The second chapter ã Foundations and State of the Art ã establishes the theoôÙ
retical and technical groundwork. It reviews the evolution of language models, the
practicalities of local inference, the RAG paradigm, and the state of the art in retrieval
engineering, augmented generation, and system evaluation.
3. The third chapter ã Data Foundation and Research Design ã details the research
methodology and data foundation. It describes the data curation pipeline, analyzes
the resulting dataset, and introduces the novel Company Truths framework as a
practical alternative to formal requirements engineering.
4. The fourth chapter ã Component Engineering and Optimization ã presents the
systematic engineering and optimization of each system component. This includes
the selection of the generative model, the programmatic tuning of prompts, the conôÙ
struction of the knowledge base, and the fineôÙtuning of text and vision embedding
models.
5. The fifth chapter ã System Integration and Performance Evaluation ã focuses
on endôÙtoôÙend system integration and benchmarking. It evaluates nine distinct RAG
architectures, comparing their effectiveness and efficiency to identify the optimal
pipeline for the final prototype.
6. The sixth chapter ã Implementation and Final Validation ã describes the impleôÙ
mentation and final validation of the ALDEVI prototype. It details the systemãs archiôÙ
tecture, its expertôÙinôÙtheôÙloop workflow, and presents the results of both qualitative
user studies and a quantitative field test on realôÙworld projects.
7. The seventh and final chapter ã Discussion, Impact, and Conclusion ã syntheôÙ
sizes the findings and discusses their broader implications. It analyzes the projectãs
strategic and financial impact for Alfa Laval, addresses its limitations, and provides
conclusions and directions for future work.

2
Foundations and State of the Art
2.1
The Evolution of Modern Language Models
The AI imperative described in the previous chapter did not emerge overnight. It is the
direct result of several foundational breakthroughs in language model technology that
have occurred over the last decade. This section traces that evolution, from the theoretical
invention of the Transformer to the rise of powerful, accessible models that make this
project technically feasible. The timeline presented in Figure 2.1 serves as a visual guide
to these key events.

Figure 2.1: Key milestones in the development of language models.
The journey began by solving a fundamental bottleneck. For years, sequential archiôÙ
tectures like Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs) and their more advanced variant, Long
ShortôÙTerm Memory Networks (LSTMs), limited progress. Their design led to slow training
and a poor grasp of longôÙrange context [6, 7]. This paradigm was broken by the ãAttenôÙ
tion Is All You Needã paper [8], which introduced the transformer architecture. Its core
innovation, the selfôÙattention mechanism, allowed the model to weigh all words in a seôÙ
quence simultaneously. This unlocked massive parallelization on Graphics Processing
Units (GPUs) and a superior understanding of context, establishing the bedrock for all
subsequent stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart models [9, 10].
The new transformer architecture immediately led to two distinct development paths. One
path focused on mastering language understanding, resulting in models such as Googleãs
Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers (BERT) [11]. BERTãs edge was
its ability to process bidirectional context, meaning it analyzes the entire sentence at once,
considering words from both the left and the right. This approach focused on deep comôÙ
prehension, which contrasted with early autoregressive models, such as Generative PreôÙ
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
6
==PAGE:21==
trained Transformer (GPT) from OpenAI. Consequently, BERTãs mastery of semantic nuôÙ
ance revolutionized tasks such as search, making the BERT architecture the foundation
for biôÙencoder embedding models, which are fundamental to the retrieval step in modern
RAG systems [12, 13].
The other path focused on mastering text generation. This research was driven by the
discovery of ãScaling Lawsã, which showed that performance improved predictably with
more data, parameters, and computational resources [14, 15]. OpenAIãs GPTôÙ3 was a
strong validation of this idea. It demonstrated a surprising emergent ability known as inôÙ
context learning, allowing it to perform complex tasks from just a few examples given in
the prompt [16]. This raw power was later packaged for the mass market with Chat GenerôÙ
ative PreôÙtrained Transformer (ChatGPT). By adding technical layers like Reinforcement
Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) and an intuitive interface, OpenAI transformed a
powerful model into a global phenomenon [17]. This success has ignited broad business
interest in AI and is one of the motivations for this project.
The final breakthrough enabling this thesis was the democratization of access to these
powerful models, a movement catalyzed by the release of Metaãs Large Language Model
Meta AI (LLaMA) [18]. For the first time, a highôÙperforming model comparable to stateôÙofôÙ
theôÙart systems was available to the broader community, igniting a wave of openôÙsource
innovation. This movement rapidly led to the development of highly efficient Small LanôÙ
guage Models (SLMs), such as Mistral 7B [19] and Microsoftãs Phi series, exemplified by
the highly capable PhiôÙ4 model [20]. These new models demonstrated that, with highôÙ
quality data, smaller models can rival the performance of much larger ones [21]. In parôÙ
allel, the openôÙsource momentum also fostered the entire ecosystem of practical tools,
including quantization formats like Generic GPT Unified Format (GGUF) and inference
servers like Ollama and llama.cpp [22, 23]. Ultimately, this democratization enables the
development and deployment of a powerful, private, and costôÙeffective AI solution tailored
to a specific industrial need, as demonstrated in this thesis.
2.2
Cloud APIs vs. Local Inference
Following the rise of the powerful foundational models discussed in the previous secôÙ
tion, the most common method for accessing their capabilities is through cloudôÙbased
Application Programming Interface (API). This approach offers significant advantages in
ease of use and reduced maintenance, as developers can integrate advanced AI without
managing complex infrastructure [24]. However, for enterprise applications that handle
sensitive information, this paradigm poses a significant challenge to data privacy. SendôÙ
ing proprietary data to thirdôÙparty servers requires legal and security agreements, such as
those provided by enterprise cloud providers like Microsoft Azure, to ensure that confidenôÙ
tial information will not be used for future model training. Consequently, navigating these
compliance requirements represents a significant hurdle, making a simple APIôÙbased apôÙ
proach unsuitable for many organizations without such enterpriseôÙgrade safeguards in
place [25].
An alternative is running models onôÙpremise. By doing so, an organization retains comôÙ
plete data sovereignty, ensuring that sensitive contractual data never leaves its secure enôÙ
vironment, thereby simplifying regulatory compliance [26]. The primary barrier to this stratôÙ
egy has historically been the immense computational requirements of LLMs. NonetheôÙ
less, the openôÙsource community has developed a robust ecosystem of tools to overcome
this challenge, making local inference practical on accessible hardware. An overview of
several key tools in this ecosystem is presented in Table 2.1.

2.2.1
The Local Inference Ecosystem
At the core of this ecosystem is llama.cpp, a highôÙperformance inference engine written in
plain C/C++ with no external dependencies. Its primary goal is to enable LLM inference
with minimal setup and stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart performance across a wide range of hardware.
It achieves this through deep optimization for various architectures, including firstôÙclass
support for Apple Silicon via Metal and NVIDIA via CUDA [23]. Moreover, it uses exôÙ
tensive integer quantization to reduce model size and accelerate computation. These
quantization schemes span from 1.5 bits to 8 bits. All quantized models are stored using
the GGUF file format, which will be described in detail later in this section.
Building on this powerful engine, tools like Ollama and LM Studio offer a more userôÙfriendly
experience. Ollama is an openôÙsource tool that simplifies running LLMs locally, packagôÙ
ing the complexity of llama.cpp into a simple commandôÙline interface and an OpenAIôÙ
compatible API server, similar to the one provided by llama.cpp [22]. For this thesis,
Ollama is particularly relevant for its streamlined support for multimodal vision models,
a task that is still in its early stages within llama.cpp. LM Studio, in contrast, offers a
Graphical User Interface (GUI) for users who prefer a desktop application for experimentôÙ
ing with different models and configurations [27].
At the other end of the spectrum is vLLM, a library that leverages PagedAttention to deliver
stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart inference speeds for production workloads [28, 29]. However, this perforôÙ
mance is highly specialized and introduces practical limitations. Notably, its multiôÙGPU
capabilities can behave unpredictably with an odd number of devices, a key considerôÙ
ation for deployment. Additionally, it does not support Apple Silicon, restricting its use
to NVIDIA and AMD GPUs. This makes vLLM a powerful but niche solution, requiring
specific hardware configurations for optimal performance. While these tools differ in their
approach, they all address the core challenge of making powerful models accessible on
a wide range of hardware.
2.2.2
Quantization and the GGUF Format
The key technology enabling this accessibility is quantization, a process standardized
by the GGUF file format. GGUF is the key technology that enables running massive
language models on accessible hardware through a process called quantization. It is a
binary file format designed for llama.cpp that bundles all necessary components into a
single, portable file, simplifying deployment significantly [30].
The power of GGUF lies in its sophisticated handling of quantization, the process of reôÙ
ducing the numerical precision of a modelãs weights (e.g., from 16ôÙbit floats to 4ôÙbit inteôÙ
gers). To maintain accuracy during this compression, it employs several advanced techôÙ
niques. The most effective methods, identified by a _K in their name, use advanced block
structures to preserve more of the modelãs original accuracy. Furthermore, sophisticated
strategies apply a ãquantization mix,ã using higher precision for more sensitive parts of the
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
8
==PAGE:23==
model and lower precision for others to achieve an optimal balance between performance
and size.
This complexity gives rise to a standardized naming convention that allows users to idenôÙ
tify each specific version. For instance, in a file named Phi-4-Q4_K_M.gguf:
ãÂ Phi-4 identifies the base model.
ãÂ Q4 signifies the primary bit depth (4ôÙbit).
ãÂ _K indicates that the advanced KôÙquant superôÙblock strategy is used.
ãÂ _M denotes a specific medium quantization mix. In this case, it means that while
most tensors are quantized to 4ôÙbit, half of the critical attention.wv and feed_forward.w2
tensors are quantized to a higher 6ôÙbit precision for improved fidelity.
2.2.3
The Performance-Resource Trade-off
Understanding these different strategies is crucial, as each quantization level creates a
model with a unique point on the performance versus resources. To quantify this tradeôÙ
off, several key metrics have been established to measure the impact of quantization on a
modelãs quality. The two primary metrics used for these evaluations are Perplexity (PPL)
and KullbackôÙLeibler Divergence (KLD).
PPL is a standard metric for evaluating language models. It measures how well a probaôÙ
bility model predicts a sample of text. A lower perplexity score indicates that the model is
less ãsurprisedã by the text, which corresponds to better predictive capabilities [31]. It is
calculated as the exponential of the crossôÙentropy loss, as shown in Equation (2.1):
PPL(P) = exp

KLD measures how one probability distribution diverges from a reference distribution.
In this context of model evaluation, it quantifies how much the output distribution of the
quantized model (Q) differs from that of the original, fullôÙprecision model (P) [32]. A lower
KLD score indicates that the quantized modelãs behavior is closer to the original. It is
defined for discrete probability distributions as:

Figure 2.2: Comparison of quantization methods by PPL, file size, and KLD [33].
The data for LLaMA 3 8B clearly illustrates the value proposition of quantization. As shown
in Figure 2.2b, moving from the original 16ôÙbit float (f16) model to more aggressive quantiôÙ
zation levels results in a dramatic reduction in model size. The file size was reduced from
14.97 GB to just 4.58 GB for the Q4_K_M model, a reduction of nearly 70%. In contrast,
the cost of performance is minimal for most of these levels. Both PPL and KLD remain
nearly flat and very low up to the Q4_K_M level. Specifically, PPL only increases from an
initial 6.23 to 6.41, an almost negligible degradation in predictive quality. Similarly, the
KLD stays below 0.06 for all quantization levels down to 4ôÙbit. The performance penalty
only becomes significant at the most extreme 3ôÙbit and 2ôÙbit levels, where the PPL curve
rises sharply past 6.78 and eventually to 9.75.
While these specific values are for the LLaMA 3 8B model, the observed trend is repreôÙ
sentative of the quantization tradeôÙoff for most modern language models. This analysis
demonstrates that a significant reduction in hardware requirements can be achieved for
a negligible cost in model quality. The ãsweet spotã for this balance generally lies in the
4ôÙbit to 6ôÙbit range, which is a remarkable outcome for deployment on consumer hardôÙ
ware. Consequently, this practical ability to run powerful models locally and securely by
leveraging the optimal tradeôÙoff offered by 4ôÙbit to 6ôÙbit quantization forms the essential
technical foundation for the RAG architecture explored in this thesis.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
10
==PAGE:25==
2.3
The Retrieval-Augmented Generation Paradigm
Having established the practicalities of deploying LLMs locally, the next critical considerôÙ
ation is the architectural strategy for integrating these models with specialized, domainôÙ
specific knowledge. There are two primary approaches to this challenge: fineôÙtuning and
RAG. FineôÙtuning refers to the process of adjusting the parameters of a preôÙtrained model
using a labeled dataset. This method is particularly suitable for adapting a modelãs style
or behavioral patterns. However, when it comes to knowledgeôÙintensive tasks, fineôÙtuning
presents several significant limitations. Specifically, it is highly resourceôÙintensive, often
requiring substantial GPU memory, extended training durations, and careful tuning of hyôÙ
perparameters. In addition, it carries the risk of catastrophic forgetting, where previously
learned information is overwritten. Moreover, because fineôÙtuning stores knowledge diôÙ
rectly in the modelãs internal parameters, updating it later is difficult and expensive. In
most cases, this requires retraining the entire model or using complex techniques that
allow the model to learn new information without forgetting what it already knows [34].
In contrast, RAG enhances a language modelãs capabilities by providing it with external
knowledge at the moment of inference. Instead of relying solely on its preôÙtrained paôÙ
rameters, a RAG system first retrieves relevant information from an external knowledge
base. Then, it uses that information as context to generate a more accurate, timely, and
verifiable response [35]. This approach offers superior scalability and memory efficiency,
as the core LLM remains unchanged while the knowledge base can grow independently.
Crucially, it reduces the likelihood of hallucinations by grounding the modelãs output in
factual, citable source material [36]. Equally important, RAG significantly reduces the
computational resources required compared to fineôÙtuning, as it eliminates the need for
retraining large models or managing expensive hardware resources. For these reasons,
RAG was selected as the core architecture for this thesis.
Recent advancements in LLMs with millionôÙtoken context windows have prompted claims
that RAG may become obsolete. However, this view overlooks critical enterprise realiôÙ
ties. Even a massive context window represents a tiny fraction of a typical companyãs
total data, and processing such large contexts introduces significant latency and compuôÙ
tational costs. Furthermore, research indicates that as context size increases, the risk of
hallucination can also rise due to an ãinformation dilution effect,ã where critical facts are
lost in a ãneedle in a haystackã problem [37]. The future, therefore, lies not in replacing
RAG but in a sophisticated convergence where RAG is used to precisely select the most
relevant information to be fed into a large context window for deeper analysis [37].
To understand how RAG functions in practice, it is helpful to break down its architecôÙ
ture into four key components: Ingestion, Retrieval, Augmentation, and Generation. This
structure is illustrated in Figure 2.3.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
11
==PAGE:26==

LLM

Figure 2.3: The fourôÙstage architecture of a RAG system.
1. Ingestion: The ingestion phase is the offline, preparatory process of building the
external knowledge base from authoritative source documents.
ãÂ Chunking: Long documents are divided into smaller, semantically meaningful
chunks to ensure that retrieval is focused and efficient.
ãÂ Embedding: An embedding model is used to convert each text chunk into a
numerical vector that represents its semantic meaning.
ãÂ Indexing: These vectors are then loaded into a vector Database (DB), which
creates an index that allows for rapid and efficient similarity searches.
2. Retrieval: This is the first step of the realôÙtime process, triggered by a user query.
ãÂ Query Embedding: The userãs query is converted into a vector using the same
embedding model from the ingestion phase.
ãÂ Similarity Search: The vector DB performs a similarity search, comparing the
query vector to the millions of indexed chunk vectors to find the most semantiôÙ
cally relevant chunks of text. These retrieved chunks form the context for the
next stage.
3. Augmentation: In this stage, the system prepares the final prompt for the LLM.
The retrieved context (i.e., relevant document chunks) is combined with the original
user query to form a structured prompt. The prompt explicitly instructs the LLM to
use the provided context to formulate its answer, thereby effectively ãaugmentingã
the modelãs knowledge with timely, relevant facts.
4. Generation: Finally, the augmented prompt is sent to the generative LLM. The
model processes the input and generates a final response that is grounded in the
retrieved information. As a result, the output remains relevant to the userãs query
and factually consistent with the source documents, offering a more accurate and
trustworthy result than what a nonôÙaugmented model could achieve.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
12
==PAGE:27==
Together, these four stages form a robust pipeline that transforms static information into
dynamic, contextually grounded responses. However, the effectiveness of this pipeline
hinges on the precision and efficiency of each step. With this foundation in place, the
following sections examine the best practices and emerging techniques for achieving highôÙ
performance RAG implementations.
2.4
State of the Art in Retrieval Engineering
The effectiveness of any RAG system is determined by the quality of its retrieval engine.
A system that cannot find the most relevant information will inevitably produce a poor
response, regardless of the LLM used. Engineering a highôÙperformance retrieval system
involves stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart techniques in both the offline ingestion phase and the realôÙtime
retrieval phase.
The first step in preparing source documents is chunking, the process of dividing long texts
into smaller, digestible pieces. The choice of chunking strategy is critical, as it directly imôÙ
pacts retrieval quality, and the optimal method often depends on the documentãs structure
and the specific domain. While simple FixedôÙSize Chunking is easy to implement, it risks
arbitrarily cutting sentences and destroying semantic meaning. To address this, more soôÙ
phisticated methods have been developed. Recursive Chunking, for example, attempts to
preserve semantic integrity by splitting text along natural boundaries like paragraphs and
sentences. Other examples include Semantic Chunking and Agentic Chunking, which
use either an embedding model or directly invoke an LLM to identify thematic breaks,
aiming to create chunks that are as coherent and selfôÙcontained as possible [38, 39]. The
effectiveness of these different strategies is typically measured using retrievalôÙspecific
metrics, which will be detailed in Section 2.6. Figure 2.4 illustrates several of these comôÙ
mon strategies, each representing a different tradeôÙoff between computational simplicity
and semantic precision.
Separating solids from liquids aids many key industrial jobs

Once documents are chunked, the next critical component in retrieval engineering is seôÙ
lecting the appropriate search architecture. Retrieval models span a spectrum of tradeôÙ
offs involving speed, computational cost, and retrieval effectiveness. As shown in TaôÙ
ble 2.2, four major paradigms dominate the landscape: sparse retrieval, dense retrieval
(biôÙencoders), crossôÙencoders, and lateôÙinteraction models. Each represents a distinct
approach to striking a balance between efficiency and accuracy in retrieving relevant inôÙ
formation.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG

ãPerformance can be significantly improved through domainôÙspecific fineôÙtuning.
As shown in the table, the journey begins with traditional sparse retrieval methods, such
as the Best Match 25 (BM25) algorithm [40]. These models operate on keyword matchôÙ
ing and term frequency, making them fast, highly scalable, and robust across domains
without any training. However, their effectiveness is limited as they cannot grasp semanôÙ
tic meaning. To capture this meaning, dense retrieval using biôÙencoder models became
the standard [41, 12]. These models use separate encoders to generate a single dense
vector for the query and each document, enabling highôÙspeed semantic search. For appliôÙ
cations requiring maximum precision, stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart systems build a multiôÙstage pipeline
that often incorporates a reôÙranking step. In this step, a more powerful model is used to reôÙ
order the initial candidates retrieved by a faster method. CrossôÙencoders offer the highest
effectiveness for this task by processing the query and a document together. However,
their slow speed makes them impractical for anything beyond reôÙranking a small set of
candidates [42, 43].
A more recent innovation, lateôÙinteraction models like Contextualized Late Interaction
over BERT (ColBERT), provides a compelling balance [44]. ColBERT independently enôÙ
codes the query and document into multiple tokenôÙlevel vectors and then performs a fineôÙ
grained similarity calculation. By performing tokenôÙlevel similarity comparisons, ColBERT
achieves effectiveness comparable to that of a crossôÙencoder while maintaining signifiôÙ
cantly better scalability. Consequently, advanced retrieval pipelines increasingly adopt
hybrid strategies, combining sparse and dense methods for broad initial retrieval, followed
by powerful lateôÙinteraction models, such as ColBERT, for precise reôÙranking.
However, a critical challenge affects all dense and lateôÙinteraction retrieval methods: the
ãdomainôÙspecificity gap.ã The term describes the issue where models trained on general
data struggle to interpret niche terminology, such as language found in specialized legal,
medical, or engineering texts. The most effective solution to this challenge involves fineôÙ
tuning the embedding model on domainôÙspecific data. FineôÙtuning exposes the model to
the unique vocabulary of the target field, which significantly enhances retrieval relevance
and overall system performance [45].
2.5
State of the Art in Augmented Generation
Once relevant context is retrieved, the final and most critical stage of the RAG pipeline
is to effectively use that information to generate a highôÙquality, grounded response. The
quality of this stage depends on prompt engineering, which involves designing inputs that
reliably produce the desired behavior from the LLM.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
14
==PAGE:29==
Early and still common approaches to prompt engineering rely on structured manual deôÙ
sign. Techniques such as fewôÙshot prompting (providing examples in the prompt), roleôÙ
based conditioning (instructing the model to act as an expert), and ChainôÙofôÙThought
(CoT) prompting form the backbone of current paradigms [46]. These strategies can
be organized into a practical framework, sometimes referred to as PersonaãPrinciplesã
Process, which involves assigning a clear identity to the model (Persona), embedding
domainôÙspecific constraints (Principles), and structuring the reasoning path (Process) [47,
48]. While effective, these manual techniques are often brittle. A prompt that works well
for one model may fail on another, and even minor changes to the task can require exôÙ
tensive and timeôÙconsuming reôÙengineering of the prompt string.
To address these limitations, modern frameworks have emerged that treat prompting not
as an art of string manipulation but as a systematic programming challenge. The most
prominent of these is DSPy, a framework that introduces a declarative, modular approach
to building and optimizing LLM pipelines [49]. As illustrated in Figure 2.5, the core idea of
DSPy is to separate the logic of a program from the specifics of the prompt. In essence, it
lets the developer define what the model should accomplish, rather than micromanaging
how the prompt should be worded. The program is defined by composing two main comôÙ
ponents: Modules, which are prompting strategies (e.g., ChainOfThought, MultiChainôÙ
Comparison), and Signatures, which act as simple blueprints for a taskãs inputs and outôÙ
puts (e.g., question ôÙ> answer). An example is shown in Listing 2.1, where a classify
Signature specifies the input (sentence) and expected outputs (sentiment, confidence),
and is instantiated in a ChainOfThought Module.

15
==PAGE:30==
If optimization is not required, DSPy compiles this highôÙlevel program directly into a funcôÙ
tional prompt for the LLM. However, its true power lies in its Optimizers. By providing a
small dataset of examples and a quality metric, a developer can use a DSPy Optimizer
to tune the pipeline automatically. The optimizer acts as a compiler, systematically genôÙ
erating and testing different prompts. For instance, it selects the best fewôÙshot examples
or rephrases instructions to produce a final, highly effective prompt tailored to the specific
model and task [50]. As a result, this programmatic approach makes AI systems more
reliable, maintainable, and portable across different models.
Optimization in DSPy is particularly valuable because static prompts tend to fail as task
complexity grows. Research shows that even advanced language models often reduce
their reasoning effort on more challenging problems, which leads to a decline in perforôÙ
mance. This phenomenon, known as the ãIllusion of Thinkingã [51], underscores the neôÙ
cessity for systems that can accommodate varying task difficulties. Although optimization
does not fully resolve this issue, it helps create pipelines that are more responsive and
aligned with task demands. As a result, DSPy offers a practical foundation for building
more robust and reliable AI applications.
2.6
Evaluation Framework for RAG Systems
Evaluating a RAG system is a complex task, as its overall performance depends on the
quality of multiple interconnected components. As outlined in the fourôÙstage architecture
introduced in Section 2.3, the system produces its output through Ingestion, Retrieval,
Augmentation, and Generation. Each of these stages contributes to the final output, but
the most critical performance factors lie in the retrieval and generation stages. The reôÙ
triever must locate the most relevant information, while the generator must integrate it
coherently and accurately. As Lewis et al. [35] demonstrate, even a strong LLM cannot
compensate for poor retrieval. This dual dependency means that a comprehensive evalôÙ
uation must be conducted in layers: first, assessing the core capabilities of the language
model, then measuring the effectiveness of the retrieval engine, and finally validating the
performance of the whole endôÙtoôÙend system on its intended task [52].
2.6.1
Evaluating Core Language Model Capabilities
The first layer of evaluation focuses on the core language model itself. Before integrating
it into an RAG pipeline, it is common practice to benchmark the general reasoning and
comprehension abilities of potential LLMs. This is typically done using a suite of standard
benchmarks. For instance, Massive Multitask Language Understanding (MMLU) tests
broad subject knowledge across topics like international law and business ethics [53].
At the same time, Stanford Question Answering Dataset (SQuAD) assesses how well
a model understands and answers factual questions from text [54]. Other benchmarks
like TruthfulQA measure a modelãs ability to avoid generating misleading statements [55],
and MathQA evaluates its capacity to solve quantitative problems [56]. Together, these
benchmarks help establish a performance baseline. However, as Rogers notes [57], they
often fail to capture the nuances of specialized, domainôÙspecific knowledge. High scores
on general tasks do not guarantee strong performance in niche applications, meaning
expert review is essential to validate a modelãs suitability for a specific field.
2.6.2
Evaluating the Retrieval Component
The second layer of evaluation focuses on the retrieval stage of the RAG pipeline. PerforôÙ
mance is typically measured using a set of standard information retrieval metrics, typically
computed over the top k results returned for each query (denoted as @k). This reflects
practical use cases where only a few context documents are passed to the generator [58].
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
16
==PAGE:31==
One of the key metrics in this context is recall@k, which quantifies how many of the truly
relevant documents appear in the top k retrieved results:
Recall@k = |relevant ãˋretrievedk|

This metric helps assess how effectively the retriever captures relevant documents, parôÙ
ticularly when completeness is essential.

appears in the list. Higher MRR indicates that relevant documents are ranked closer to
the top, improving response speed and model grounding:

In addition to these core metrics, other metrics may be used depending on the setup.
Precision@k measures the fraction of the top k retrieved relevant documents, which
can be helpful when minimizing irrelevant results is a priority. Mean Average Precision
(MAP@k) averages precision across all relevant documents and queries and is helpful
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
17
==PAGE:32==
when pertinent multiple documents may be present. Finally, when evaluating document
chunking strategies during ingestion, Intersection over Union (IoU) can be used to meaôÙ
sure how well a retrieved chunk overlaps with a gold answer span [59]. For completeness,
detailed formulas for precision@k, Mean Average Precision (MAP), DCG, and IoU can be
found in Appendix A.
2.6.3
Evaluating the End-to-End System
The third and final layer of evaluation addresses the full RAG pipeline operating endôÙ
toôÙend. The focus shifts from individual components to whether the integrated system
achieves its intended business purpose. In this thesis, the systemãs purpose of detecting
contractual deviations is framed as a binary classification problem. Evaluation, therefore,
relies on comparing the systemãs final predictions to an expertôÙannotated ground truth
using standard classification metrics derived from the confusion matrix:
ãÂ Accuracy: The proportion of all correct predictions.
ãÂ Precision: The fraction of items flagged as deviations that were deviations.
ãÂ Recall: The fraction of all true deviations that the system successfully identified.
ãÂ F1 score: The harmonic mean of precision and recall provides a single, balanced
measure of performance.
In this context, recall emerges as the most critical metric. A high recall, or True Positive
Rate (TPR), is essential because the business cost of a false negative, defined as failôÙ
ing to identify a true deviation, can result in significant compliance or legal risk. While
achieving a high recall is a crucial quantitative benchmark, numerical metrics alone are
often insufficient for professional adoption in complex domains. For a system to be truly
effective, it must also be trustworthy and explainable.
Consequently, modern evaluation frameworks place a high value on traceability, the sysôÙ
temãs ability to cite the exact source document and passage for its findings [60]. Such a
capacity for verification is critical for building user trust, as it empowers domain experts
to validate the systemãs reasoning and maintain ultimate control over the process. For
these reasons, traceability stands as a key success factor for any RAG system deployed
in a highôÙstakes environment.
2.7
Alternative Solutions and Market Landscape
While this thesis focuses on engineering an RAG solution, it is important to contextuôÙ
alize this work within the broader market. Existing commercial solutions generally fall
into two categories: Enterprise Search Platforms (ESP), designed for general informaôÙ
tion retrieval, and more focused Contract Lifecycle Management (CLM) tools intended for
legal and commercial workflows. Platforms like Microsoft 365 Copilot [61] excel at creôÙ
ating a semantic index across an organizationãs entire data footprint, enabling powerful
enterpriseôÙwide search. In parallel, CLM tools such as Ironclad [62], Evisort [63], top.legal
[64], and Legitt AI [65] focus on the legal domain, offering features like automated clause
extraction and AIôÙpowered redlining suggestions (see Table 2.3).

deployment through quantization, the strategic choice of the RAG architecture, and the
specific market need for a domainôÙaware solution. With this context in place, the followôÙ

3.2
Data Curation
The primary data source for this research is a collection of historical project files stored
on a corporate OneDrive. Although records date back to 2009, significant changes in
company standards and issues with data quality required limiting the scope of projects
from 2018 to February 2025. This dataset is essential, as it provides the ground truth for
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
21
==PAGE:36==
the study: thousands of realôÙworld contractual deviations that were identified and comôÙ
mented on by domain experts at Alfa Laval. These deviations are documented in internal
review files, which are typically in Microsoft Excel format. Each review file corresponds
to a specific project and is accompanied by the original customer documentation, which
may vary in format. Both the review content and its structure are based on a master temôÙ
plate maintained by the E&S department. However, this template has evolved over time,
and engineers frequently adapt it to meet projectôÙspecific needs, resulting in significant
inconsistencies across the dataset.
To supplement this primary historical data, a secondary corpus of internal reference docuôÙ
ments was also assembled. This collection includes official corporate standards, product
operator manuals, installation manuals, and content from internal SharePoint pages. UnôÙ
like the historical files, these documents are formally published and wellôÙstructured.
Together, these two distinct corpora (the historical project data and the internal reference
documents) provide the raw material for engineering the CT knowledge base that will be
described in Section 4.3. They serve complementary purposes:
ãÂ Historical Data provides a source of implicit, fieldôÙtested knowledge. The expert
comments in the deviation logs are analyzed to identify common patterns, stanôÙ
dards, and practices employed in realôÙworld projects.
ãÂ Reference Documents provide a source of explicit, documented knowledge. These
include formal company standards and technical specifications that can be directly
converted into clear, verifiable rules.
The reference documents were collected through a combination of manual downloads
and extractions from internal SharePoint sites, as well as direct access to corporate stanôÙ
dard repositories. Given their formally published and wellôÙstructured nature, no further
programmatic cleaning was required beyond basic text extraction.
The central data engineering challenge was converting the unstructured historical records
into a format that could be analyzed. To overcome it, a dedicated processing pipeline
consisting of two sequential phases was implemented.
3.2.1

into a single, consistent directory structure. The automated pipeline, illustrated in FigôÙ

The explicitôÙknowledge corpus consists of 69 corporate standards (each averaging about

words of formal guidance, underscoring the value of combining realôÙworld practices with
documented standards. These sources form a solid foundation. The next section examôÙ

3.4
Limits of Formal Requirements
Before developing a custom knowledge framework, the logical first step was to adopt
the established industry standards for requirements engineering. This section deconôÙ
structs a practical framework for formal requirements, primarily based on ISO/IEC/IEEE
29148:2018 [66], supplemented by the INCOSE Systems Engineering Handbook [67] and
the NASA Systems Engineering Handbook [68]. While theoretically robust and broadly
endorsed across software, hardware, and systems engineering contexts, this suite of
standards proved ultimately unsuitable for Alfa Lavalãs specific operational context, a conôÙ
clusion that our empirical validation later confirms (Section 5.2).
3.4.1
Definition and Structure
According to the standards, a requirement is a statement that expresses a specific need
along with any constraints or conditions, and it must be precise, unambiguous, and diôÙ
rectly tied to the system, component, or service in question [66]. Working with Alfa Laval
experts, we distilled these principles into the prescriptive template shown in the box below
to guarantee that every requirement is clear, testable, and necessary across all domains.
Formal Requirement Structure
The [component/system/company] shall [perform action/function]
under
[condition/situation] to [achieve specific outcome] with [performance criteôÙ
ria] in compliance with [relevant standard/specification].
This template mirrors the ISO guidance by using the mandatory verb ãshallã to express
obligation unambiguously. It identifies the responsible party, defines the required action,
specifies the conditions, the intended outcome, and includes performance criteria to enôÙ
sure verifiability. Referencing relevant standards adds traceability and consistency across
contexts.
3.4.2
Classification and Validation
To ensure wellôÙstructured requirements and enable systematic deviation detection, a tenôÙ
category classification system was developed based on relevant literature [69, 70] and
refined through consultation with Alfa Laval experts. While ISO 29148 guides the formuôÙ
lation of individual requirements, it does not define a scheme for organizing them. Our
taxonomy fills this gap by covering the full range of deviation types encountered in pracôÙ
tice. The categories and their defining criteria are listed in Table 3.2.
Table 3.2: Classification taxonomy for formal requirements.
Category
Definition Criteria
Technical
Discrepancies in work execution, design, or specifications.
Cost
Variations in project costs, pricing, or financial terms.
Time
Delays or changes in project timeline or milestones.
Quality
Failure to meet specified quality standards or requirements.
Legal
NonôÙcompliance with contractual terms or applicable laws.
Administrative
Errors or omissions in contract administration or documentation.
Scope
Changes or discrepancies in project scope or deliverables.
Performance
Failure to meet specified performance criteria or standards.
Safety
NonôÙcompliance with safety regulations or standards.
Communication
Failures in required reporting, documentation, or information sharing.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
26
==PAGE:41==
By grouping potential nonôÙconformances into Technical, Cost, Time, Quality, Legal, AdôÙ
ministrative, Scope, Performance, Safety, and Communication categories, this scheme
ensures broad coverage across Alfa Lavalãs project lifecycle. It also enables focused
validation by aligning checks with the most relevant stakeholders.
Beyond classification, the quality of each requirement must be rigorously assessed. The
ISO 29148 standard provides a stringent set of validation criteria to ensure that every
requirement is robust, transparent, and fit for purpose. These criteria, presented in TaôÙ
ble 3.3, serve as a checklist for authoring highôÙquality requirements.
Table 3.3: Validation criteria for formal requirements (ISO/IEC/IEEE 29148:2018).
Criterion
Description
Necessary
Essential capability or constraint whose absence causes an unfilled deficiency.
Appropriate
Detail and abstraction levels suit the entity without imposing design constraints.
Unambiguous
Can be interpreted in only one wayëƒ clear and easy to understand.
Complete
Fully describes the capability, constraint, or quality factor without omissions.
Singular
States exactly one capability, characteristic, constraint, or quality factor.
Feasible
Realizable within cost, schedule, and technical constraints with acceptable risk.
Verifiable
Defined so that its fulfillment can be objectively proven or measured.
Correct
Faithfully represents the original need from which it was derived.
Conforming
Written following the approved standard template and style guidelines.
Although these criteria establish a robust checklist for highôÙquality requirements, their rigid
application in Alfa Lavalãs complex operational environment uncovered practical gaps and
inefficiencies.
3.4.3
Limitations in Practice
While this formal framework provides a theoretically sound model for clarity and rigor, its
application to the data in Section 3.2 revealed significant practical limitations. The attempt
to retroactively fit expert comments into this rigid structure proved to be a primary source of
information loss and, as the empirical results in Section 5.2 will demonstrate, poor system
performance. The key limitations identified were threefold:
1. The Rigidity Problem: The formal ãshallã structure is too rigid for many of Alfa
Lavalãs internal standards, which are often flexible ãstandard practicesã rather than
absolute rules. More importantly, it cannot naturally handle negative constraints.
A critical business rule, such as ãWe do not offer extended warranties beyond five
years,ã is essential for review but does not fit the ãshall doã format, making the frameôÙ
work incomplete for realôÙworld use.
2. The Context Problem: The frameworkãs demand for unambiguous and singular
statements strips away crucial business context. A typical scenario is a customer
requesting an optional upgrade. An expertãs note, ãThe customer wants the preôÙ
mium coating system, which is an upgrade,ã is not a simple deviation but a change
in scope with cost implications. A formal requirement cannot capture this ãbase vs.
upgradeã logic, losing the commercial context needed to prompt an effective AI.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
27
==PAGE:42==
3. The Feasibility Problem: Adopting this framework would require a significant orôÙ
ganizational shift. Training the engineers to consistently think, write, and validate
thousands of nuanced practices according to this rigid, nineôÙpoint standard would
be a massive and costly undertaking.
These practical shortcomings, together with the poor performance of the requirementsôÙ
based system, made it clear that a different approach was needed. The standard frameôÙ
work, while theoretically sound, is fundamentally misaligned with the way expert knowlôÙ
edge is captured and applied at Alfa Laval. This finding required the development of a
more flexible and contextôÙaware knowledge representation, as detailed in the following
section.
3.5
The Company Truths Framework
The practical limitations of the formal requirements framework necessitated the develôÙ
opment of a more flexible and contextôÙaware alternative.
In close collaboration with
Alfa Lavalãs domain experts, a novel knowledge representation, known as the Company
Truth, was engineered. This framework is designed to capture the nuanced reality of
internal standards and expert knowledge in a way that is both verifiable by humans and
readily usable by an AI system.
3.5.1
Definition and Structure
A CT is defined as a clear and precise statement of internal practice, constraint, or decision
that affects compliance with customer requirements and guides internal verification.
Unlike the rigid ãshallã template of formal requirements, the structure of a CT is flexible. It
is designed to be a succinct, standalone sentence composed from a set of building blocks,
using only those that apply in each specific case. This flexibility is key to overcoming the
Rigidity Problem identified in Section 3.4.3.
Company Truth Building Blocks
Who/what: [company/component/system]
Action: [complies with/specifies/ensures/declares/does not include/etc.]
Subject: [requirement/practice/item/process]
Condition: [under/according to/in compliance with / etc.] [standard/condition]
Purpose (optional): to [achieve/satisfy/support] [goal]
Performance criteria/details (optional): with [criteria/details]
This flexible structure enables the natural expression of diverse types of business knowlôÙ
edge. For example, it can easily handle the negative constraints that the formal framework
struggled with: [Alfa Laval] [does not include] [extended warranties] [beyond five years].
Similarly, it can capture the ãbase vs. upgradeã logic that was lost in the formal approach,
directly addressing the Context Problem.
3.5.2
Classification and Validation
To organize the CTs, a custom classification taxonomy was developed. Rather than relyôÙ
ing on a generic standard, this taxonomy was derived ãbottomôÙupã by analyzing the table of
contents from historical project documents and using an LLM to suggest common themes.
These themes were then refined and validated by Alfa Lavalãs experts. This process reôÙ
sulted in the ten practical categories shown in Table 3.4, which are directly aligned with
the companyãs operational structure.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
28
==PAGE:43==

Focuses on pricing strategies, cost analysis, currency manôÙ

tion, and deadline management.

and industry regulations.

Although the classification taxonomy was essential for organizing the knowledge base,
it was not used to inform the prototypeãs analytical logic, a limitation whose implications,
along with the potential for a more practical, departmentôÙbased classification, are anaôÙ
lyzed in detail in Section 7.4.
In addition to a practical classification scheme, the framework required an equally practical
validation process. To directly address the Feasibility Problem identified with the formal
requirements approach, the comprehensive nineôÙpoint validation checklist was streamôÙ
lined. The goal was to retain the most critical quality criteria while eliminating the excesôÙ
sive authoring burden that would create an organizational blockage. As a result, a core
set of five essential criteria was defined, as shown in Table 3.5.
Table 3.5: Streamlined validation criteria for CTs.
Criterion
Description
Necessary
Captures an essential internal practice, decision, or constraint.
Unambiguous
Clearly and factually stated with only one possible interpretation.
Complete
Fully describes the practice or decision without needing additional context.
Singular
Focuses on one core idea, even if multiple aspects are mentioned.
Verifiable
Structured so its validity can be confirmed against internal or customer docuôÙ
mentation.
In summary, the CT framework, grounded in a flexible structure, a customôÙderived taxôÙ
onomy, and a streamlined validation process, provides a robust yet efficient solution for
capturing the complex realities of industrial engineering knowledge.

3.6
Key Takeaways from Data Foundation
The results in this chapter form the basis for all further stages of the research. The main
conclusions are as follows:
ãÂ A Substantial, Curated Dataset: Transforming an extensive collection of 456 project
folders into a reliable dataset required a robust curation pipeline. Despite excluding
many projects due to quality concerns, the final dataset of 182 valid projects, conôÙ
taining 2,610 deviation records, provides a substantial and representative foundaôÙ
tion for this study.
ãÂ The Limitations of Standard Frameworks: IndustryôÙstandard methods for docuôÙ
menting requirements, with their rigid ãshallã structures and strict validation criteria,
proved unsuitable for Alfa Lavalãs operational reality. These frameworks often fail
to capture the nuanced, contextôÙdependent manner in which expert knowledge is
recorded and applied.
ãÂ A Practical, Alternative Framework: Collaboration with domain experts led to the
creation of a more flexible approach: the Company Truths framework. This novel
method is designed to reflect both formal policies and informal expert insights, reôÙ
sulting in a more comprehensive and usable representation of company knowledge.
ãÂ A Balanced Knowledge Foundation: The methodology successfully integrates
two complementary sources of knowledge: the implicit, fieldôÙtested expertise found
in the historical deviation records, and the explicit, documented standards from the
reference corpus. This balance ensures the integrity and practical relevance of the
data foundation.
With these achievements, all requirements for Gate 1 ã Data Foundation have been
fulfilled. The research can now move forward to Gate 2 ã Component Engineering,
where technical development and system optimization become the primary focus.

4
Component Engineering and
Optimization
4.1
Generation Engine
The efficacy of any RAG system depends on the capabilities of its underlying LLM. As
established in Section 2.2 and Section 1.5, this PoC is constrained by data privacy, hardôÙ
ware, and feasibility considerations, requiring models suitable for efficient local inference.
This section details the process for selecting the most appropriate LLM for ALDEVI.
The process began by identifying a set of highôÙperforming, openôÙsource LLMs that supôÙ
port quantization in the GGUF format and can run efficiently on consumerôÙgrade hardware.
Candidate models were selected based on performance rankings from the Hugging Face
Open LLM Leaderboard [71] and Artificial Analysis Leaderboards [72]. In addition to perôÙ
formance and accessibility (as of early 2025), we also considered the practical constraints
of the evaluation environment, including limited memory and processing power. The folôÙ
lowing candidates were selected for rigorous benchmarking:
ãÂ DeepSeekR1-Distill-Qwen-14B-Q4_K_M
ãÂ Mistral-Small-24B-Instruct-2501-Q2_K
ãÂ Meta-Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct-Q6_K_L
ãÂ Phi-4-Q4_K_M
The quantization levels (Q4_K_M, Q2_K, Q6_K_L) reflect a balance between model accuôÙ
racy and the ability to run on resourceôÙlimited systems, as discussed in Section 2.2.3.
Notably, all candidates at this stage are textôÙonly models, reflecting the projectãs twoôÙ
phase plan and the limits of the MacBook Air used for initial testing with sensitive Alfa
Laval data. Multimodal models were deferred to the systemôÙlevel phase on the multiôÙ
GPU workstation. The selection process is detailed in Section 5.3.
To objectively assess the general reasoning and comprehension abilities of these candiôÙ
date models, a comprehensive benchmarking suite was employed using the DeepEval
framework [73]. The selected benchmarks and subtests reflect the range of reasoning,
factual, and quantitative skills needed for contract analysis and technical document review
in industrial domains. In particular, legal, technical, and financial topics were prioritized,
as these best match the language and reasoning challenges encountered at Alfa Laval.
The evaluation included the following benchmarks and subtests:
ãÂ MMLU: Assesses broad subject knowledge and logical reasoning across various
disciplines, including subtests relevant to legal and technical domains such as busiôÙ
ness ethics, electrical engineering, international law, professional law, jurisprudence,
and management.
ãÂ SQuAD: Measures factual comprehension and the ability to extract answers from
text passages, with topics including: intergovernmental panel on climate change,
european union law, steam engines, packet switching, the Apollo program, and
computational complexity theory. For this benchmark, o3ôÙmini was used as the
LLM judge.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
31
==PAGE:46==
ãÂ TruthfulQA: Evaluates a modelãs ability to generate accurate and nonôÙhallucinatory
responses, focusing mainly on topics in law and finance.
ãÂ MathQA: Tests quantitative reasoning and problemôÙsolving skills in general matheôÙ
matics and physics.
Given that this initial benchmarking phase did not involve sensitive Alfa Laval data, the
evaluations were conducted on a more powerful cloudôÙbased GPU environment (an A100
SXM on RunPod at $1.89/h [74]). As a result, the benchmarking process was both efficient
and practical, whereas comprehensive testing on local hardware would have significantly
increased evaluation time.

in Table 4.1. The table includes average scores for each benchmark category, based on

The benchmark results indicate that Phi-4 delivers consistently strong performance across
all evaluated metrics. It achieved the highest scores in MMLU (0.66) and MathQA (0.36),
reflecting robust general reasoning and quantitative abilities. Its scores in SQuAD (0.92)
and TruthfulQA (0.62) were also among the best, demonstrating reliable comprehension
and factual accuracy. While Mistral-Small also exhibited high scores, outperforming
in some benchmarks such as SQuAD and TruthfulQA, the differences are relatively minor.
Notably, Phi-4 maintained competitive efficiency, with a total processing time of 4683.97
seconds, suggesting that its quality does not come at excessive computational cost.
However, because both models performed similarly on benchmarks, further validation
with domain experts is needed to determine which one best fits the specific demands of
contract analysis at Alfa Laval. Expert input is crucial for evaluating how effectively each
model handles domainôÙspecific language and reasoning that generic benchmarks may
overlook.
4.1.2
Expert Validation Pipeline
To ensure the chosen language model met the demands of Alfa Lavalãs domain, a strucôÙ
tured expert validation pipeline was developed.
This pipeline used DSPyãs modular,
signatureôÙbased prompt programming to define clear interfaces for each task. Within each
DSPy Signature, the 3Ps paradigm (Persona, Principles, and Process) was explicitly inôÙ
corporated into the design of the prompts. Two core DSPy signatures were implemented:
one for the extraction of CTs and another for their classification, with complete signature
definitions provided in Appendix C.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
32
==PAGE:47==
Both Phi-4 and Mistral-Small were integrated into this pipeline and applied to the
contract template referenced earlier (see Section 3.2). For each example, the extracôÙ
tion and classification signatures generated candidate CTs, which were anonymized and
presented side by side to Alfa Laval domain experts for blind evaluation. Experts asôÙ
sessed each pair for accuracy and clarity, selecting the preferred output in each case.
The results were clear: Phi-4 was chosen in 11 out of 13 instances. Reviewers noted
that Mistral-Smallãs outputs were more prone to hallucinations and logical inconsisôÙ
tencies, likely due to the Q2 quantization level. In contrast, Phi-4 consistently produced
more precise and reliable CTs. While some minor edits were occasionally required, its
overall quality and trustworthiness made it the preferred model. An illustrative example of
this expert validation is shown in Table 4.2.

4.2
Prompt-Program Optimization
With Phi-4-Q4_K_M selected as the core generation engine for ALDEVI, the next step
in Gate 2 ã Component Engineering is to optimize it for CT extraction and classification.
As discussed in Section 2.5, manual prompt design is brittle and timeôÙconsuming. This
section introduces a programmatic approach using DSPy, which turns static prompts into
learnable programs tailored to Alfa Lavalãs domain.
DSPy was selected for its declarative design, which decouples pipeline logic from the unôÙ
derlying prompt strings. This enables iterative, automatic prompt tuning via optimizers, a
clear advantage over manual engineering. Tasks are specified through DSPy Signatures,
which formalize inputs and outputs. The system then learns, through optimization, how
to best construct effective prompts and select relevant examples.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
33
==PAGE:48==
The optimization was grounded in the expertôÙvalidated dataset described above, which
includes 15 extraction examples (mapping expert comments to zero, one, or more CTs)
and 36 classification examples (each CT labeled according to the custom taxonomy
in Section 3.5.2). This dataset was split 40/60 into training and testing subsets, as is
standard for promptôÙlevel optimization, despite this approach contrasting with typical deep
learning practices.

The process of optimizing prompt programs for CT extraction and classification followed
a systematic workflow, illustrated in Figure 4.1. This diagram summarizes the full optiôÙ
mization pipeline, from starting with expertôÙvalidated examples through data splitting to
benchmarking multiple optimizers and finally selecting the optimal program.

Within this workflow, several DSPy optimizers were benchmarked to identify the most
effective program for each task:
ãÂ LabeledFewShot: Randomly inserts k expertôÙlabeled inputãoutput pairs from the
training set as demonstrations.
ãÂ BootstrapFewShot: A teacher LLM synthesises candidate demos, keeps those
that satisfy the metric, and mixes them with real examples.
ãÂ BSFewShotôÙRS (BootstrapFewShot with Random Search): Runs BootstrapFewôÙ
Shot multiple times with shuffled demo sets and keeps the highestôÙscoring variant.
ãÂ KNNFewShot: Dynamically selects the kôÙnearest neighbor examples from the trainôÙ
ing set (via embeddings) for each new input.
Several advanced optimizers (CoPRO, MIPROv2, BootstrapFinetune, and Ensemble)
were considered but ultimately excluded due to their risk of altering expertôÙdefined strucôÙ
tures, computational demands, or suboptimal performance on small datasets (as per the
DSPy documentation [49]).

CT Classification: For the classification task, accuracy was measured by an exact match
between the predicted category and the expertôÙassigned ground truth for each CT.
CT Extraction: Evaluating extracted CTs required a more nuanced approach, as a single
expert comment can lead to multiple valid CTs. To address this, a custom evaluation
function was implemented to assess both quality and semantic fidelity across several
dimensions:
1. LLM Validation: Provided by Phi-4 itself (acting as an impartial ãcriticã), this score
assessed how well each extracted CT adhered to the validation criteria for CTs
outlined in Table 3.5. Specifically, it evaluated whether the extracted CT was NecôÙ
essary, Unambiguous, Complete, Singular, and Verifiable.
2. Cosine Similarity: Calculated between the embedding of the extracted CT and
the original expert comment, this metric ensured the retention of the core semantic
meaning.
3. Count Comparison: A measure of how well the number of extracted CTs matched
the number of ground truth CTs for a given expert comment.
These components were combined into a single extraction quality score. The final score
was calculated using weighted averages: 70% for the LLMôÙjudged quality score, 20% for
the count comparison, and 10% for cosine similarity. This weighting prioritizes the LLMôÙ
based assessment, as it best reflects how well the output aligns with expert expectations,
while also accounting for semantic similarity and the completeness of the extracted set.
The whole optimization process (extraction/classification) was computationally intensive,
requiring approximately 30 hours on the initial local machine (MacBook Air M3 15ôÙinch
with 16GB of RAM). This significant duration underscores both the feasibility of promptôÙ
program tuning in constrained environments and the inherent limits of such setups (as
further elaborated in Section 5.1).

4.3
Building the Company Truths Knowledge Base
The CT knowledge base serves as the authoritative reference for assessing deviations
in customer requirements. It directly supports the ãKnowledge Foundationã pillar of this
research (Section 1.3) and enables systematic retrieval within the RAG pipeline. To enôÙ
sure comprehensive coverage, it integrates two complementary sources: implicit insights
mined from historical deviation records and explicit guidance codified in internal docuôÙ
mentation (see Section 3.3).
4.3.1

To systematically convert past project expertise into structured knowledge, the historical
deviation dataset is processed through a multiôÙstage pipeline. This approach transforms
expert commentary into highôÙquality CT candidates, forming the backbone of the knowlôÙ
edge base. The pipeline is illustrated in Figure 4.3 and described below.
