1. Extraction and Classification
Given the validated LLM configuration and prompt programs (Section 4.1 and Section 4.2),
the entire set of historical deviation records (2,610) was processed. Each expert comment
was analyzed by the DSPy extraction and classification signatures, generating a large set
of atomic CT statements, each automatically assigned to one of ten semantic categories.
In total, this procedure produced 5,378 CT statements. This step, although computaôÙ
tionally demanding (taking over 18 hours on the MacBook Air M3), ensured broad and
systematic coverage of the implicit knowledge present in the historical corpus.
2. Redundancy and Patterns
An initial review conducted both manually and with domain experts revealed high deôÙ
grees of redundancy and subtle variations in the extracted CTs. Many expressed similar
underlying company positions but with different wording or levels of specificity. Moreover,
recurring deviations across projects suggested a set of baseline, companyôÙwide ãtruths.ã
Therefore, a robust clustering step was needed to group semantically similar CTs, and
minimize duplication.
3. Dimensionality Reduction and Embedding Selection
Clustering directly in the full highôÙdimensional embedding space (n = 5378) is compuôÙ
tationally intensive and can obscure interpretable structures, particularly when the numôÙ
ber of clusters is large. To address this, dimensionality reduction was applied prior to
clustering, with the goals of reducing computational load, improving cluster quality, and
preserving meaningful data geometry.
Each CT was embedded using a suite of openôÙsource models based on the transformer
architecture, selected for their strong performance on public benchmarks (notably the
Massive Text Embedding Benchmark (MTEB) leaderboard [75, 76]) and their practicality
for local inference:
ãÂ all-MiniLM-L6-v2
ãÂ all-mpnet-base-v2
ãÂ multilingual-e5-large-instruct
ãÂ qwen2-1.5B-instruct
ãÂ qwen2-7B-instruct-Q5_K_M
ãÂ GritLM-7B-Q5_K_M
To guide the number of clusters k, we explored the range [1400, 2200], based on expert
judgment and initial inspection of clustering results, aiming to balance granularity with
interpretability.
Two leading dimensionality reduction techniques were then systematically compared:
Principal Component Analysis (PCA) and Uniform Manifold Approximation and Projection
(UMAP). For PCA, we retained the minimum number of components required to preserve
95% of the original variance. For UMAP, a grid search was conducted to tune parameters
such as n_neighbors and min_dist, resulting in a configuration (n_neighbors=50,
min_dist=0.05) optimized for global structure preservation and tight cluster formation.
To assess the fidelity of these reductions, we evaluated trustworthiness, neighbor overlap
(measured by Jaccard similarity), and pairwise distance correlation using Spearmanãs
rank correlation. Both the trustworthiness and Jaccard similarity metrics were computed
using k = 30 nearest neighbors. This value of k is independent of the clustering parameter
k used elsewhere in this thesis. Results are summarized in Table 4.4.

As shown in Table 4.4, PCA outperforms UMAP across all structural preservation metrics.
It nearly perfectly maintains both local neighborhood relationships and global distance corôÙ

Although UMAP remains useful for 2D visualization, PCA offers a more faithful and stable
representation of the embedding space, which is essential for reliable clustering.
Standard clustering quality indices, including Silhouette, DaviesãBouldin, CalinskiãHaraôÙ

puted after applying KôÙMeans clustering to each reduced space. Results for the Silhouette

Across all tested values of k, PCA consistently outperformed the original space in terms
of Silhouette and Dunn scores, indicating the practical preservation of cluster structure.
UMAP, by contrast, showed a clear downward trend in Silhouette performance as k inôÙ
creased. Despite UMAPãs steep rise in the CalinskiãHarabasz index, this was interpreted
as an artifact of overôÙfragmentation, reflecting tightly packed clusters in a distorted space
rather than actual improvements in clustering quality.
While UMAP remains valuable for lowôÙdimensional visualization and discovery of local
manifolds, its tendency to warp global geometry introduces significant risks when used
as a basis for clustering. PCA, on the other hand, provides a stable, reproducible, and
interpretable dimensionality reduction that preserves both local and global relationships,
which are essential for reliable clustering. For these reasons, PCA was selected as the
standard method for dimensionality reduction.

==PAGE:53==
4. Clustering Algorithms and Hyperparameter Selection
With the dimensionality reduction method (PCA) established, the next phase involved
evaluating various clustering algorithms to group the extracted CTs optimally. The objecôÙ
tive was to identify clusters of semantically similar CTs that could then be consolidated,
targeting a final cluster count within the previously established range of 1400 to 2200.
A systematic evaluation was conducted across several prominent clustering algorithms,
each chosen for its distinct approach to data partitioning:
ãÂ KôÙMeans (using MiniBatchKMeans for performance): The number of clusters (k)
was varied within the target range of 1400 to 2200. This method served as the
baseline due to its simplicity and scalability.
ãÂ HDBSCAN: As a densityôÙbased method, Hierarchical DensityôÙBased Spatial ClusôÙ
tering of Applications with Noise (HDBSCAN) was tuned by adjusting min_clu-
ster_size (2, 3), min_samples (1, 2), and cluster_selection_method (ãeomã,
ãleafã). These settings determine the density of points required to form a cluster, with
tuning aimed at achieving a cluster count within the target range.
ãÂ Agglomerative Clustering: This hierarchical approach was tested using various
distance_threshold values (0.85 to 1.1) and ward linkage with Euclidean disôÙ
tance. Since the algorithm does not require a fixed number of clusters, the threshold
was used to approximate the desired cluster count.
ãÂ Gaussian Mixture Model (GMM): Evaluated using n_components from 1400 to
2200, mirroring the values tested for KôÙMeans. As a probabilistic method, it assigns
soft cluster memberships, which can help capture overlapping semantic concepts.
To ensure the robustness and reliability of the clustering results, each configuration (where
applicable, particularly for nonôÙdeterministic algorithms like KôÙMeans and GMM) was exôÙ
ecuted three times with different random seeds, and the average performance metrics
were recorded.
For objectively comparing the performance of these diverse clustering algorithms on texôÙ
tual data, a custom Clustering Composite Quality Index (CCQI) was developed. This
index combines five widely recognized clustering metrics, each weighted to reflect its imôÙ
portance in assessing both the statistical quality and semantic coherence of CT clusters.
The CCQI formula is defined as:
CCQI = 0.40 Silhouette + 0.20 CalinskiôÙHarabasz
+ 0.15 DaviesôÙBouldininv + 0.05 Dunn + 0.20 NPMI
(4.1)
Each component of the CCQI was selected for its relevance to clustering quality and
weighted accordingly. Silhouette (0.40) prioritizes clusters that are wellôÙseparated and
internally consistent, which is essential for capturing distinct semantic groups. Calinskiã
Harabasz (0.20) evaluates overall cluster compactness and separation. DaviesãBouldin
(0.15) measures average similarity between clustersëƒ its inverse is used so that higher
values reflect better distinction. The Dunn Index (0.05) considers the ratio of interôÙcluster
separation to intraôÙcluster compactness but receives a lower weight due to its sensitivity
to outliers. Finally, Normalized Pointwise Mutual Information (NPMI) (0.20) assesses seôÙ
mantic coherence within each cluster, a critical factor for textual data. All metrics were
normalized to a 0ã1 scale across configurations to ensure balanced and unbiased comôÙ
parisons when computing the final CCQI.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
39
==PAGE:54==
The evaluation of all embedding models and clustering algorithm combinations, ranked
by Global CCQI, is summarized in Appendix Table D.2, with a detailed metric breakdown
in Appendix Table D.1. Across all tested embeddings, Agglomerative Clustering conôÙ
sistently produced the highest scores. Its hierarchical linkage strategy proved particularly
effective at grouping the diverse and often subtly related CTs into semantically coherôÙ
ent clusters. The top three configurations, all based on Agglomerative Clustering, were
multilingual-e5-large-instruct (Global CCQI = 0.967), all-mpnet-base-v2
(0.959), and GritLM-7B-Q5_K_M (0.948).
Although the multilingual-e5-large-instruct delivered the highest score, its size
(approximately 560 million parameters) made it impractical for fineôÙtuning on the available
hardware. Since downstream tasks in the RAG pipeline required taskôÙspecific adaptation
(see Section 4.4), we selected a more lightweight alternative that still offered strong perforôÙ
mance. The all-mpnet-base-v2 model, with around 109 million parameters, met this
requirement. It supported efficient local fineôÙtuning and achieved nearôÙoptimal clustering
quality. As a result, we adopted Agglomerative Clustering with allôÙmpnetôÙbaseôÙv2 for
grouping the CTs, and designated it as the default embedding model for all subsequent
stages. This configuration generated 2,342 clusters from the 5,378 CTs extracted from
historical deviations, serving as the input to the final consolidation phase.
5. CT Candidates
Following the identification of optimal clusters, the final step in mining historical data was
to synthesize the extracted CTs into a more concise and representative set. A new DSPy
Signature was defined (see Appendix Listing C.3) and applied to each cluster, with the
grouped CT statements passed back into the Phi-4 LLM. The model was instructed to
generate a single, concise candidate that captured the collective meaning of the cluster
while strictly adhering to the validation criteria outlined in Section 3.5.2. This process sigôÙ
nificantly reduced redundancy, converting many variations into a streamlined set of canonôÙ
ical CT candidates. Moreover, analyzing cluster sizes revealed which CTs appeared most
frequently in historical projects, highlighting recurring patterns and core practices. The reôÙ
sult was a wellôÙstructured, expertôÙgrounded knowledge base that forms a key part of the
overall CT foundation.
4.3.2
Knowledge Mining from Internal Documentation
Complementing the implicit knowledge mined from historical deviations, a second key
source of CTs is the corpus of formal internal reference documents. As described in
Section 3.3.2, these include corporate standards, SharePoint pages, and product manuôÙ
als. For this phase, the focus was on 143 SharePoint pages and their linked documents,
since they offer the most current and practically relevant insights into ongoing engineering
practices. Corporate standards were set aside for verification in a later stage. This explicit
knowledge is crucial because it provides codified and officially approved guidelines along
with technical specifications. As a result, it helps ground the knowledge base in both
documented policy and realôÙworld observations. The multiôÙstage pipeline used to extract
CTs from the SharePoint collection, shown in Figure 4.5, follows the same conceptual
structure as the historical data pipeline and is summarized below.

1. Document Processing
The first step involved robustly extracting clean, structured text from the diverse formats
of the SharePoint documents. For this, a combination of specialized libraries was emôÙ
ployed to handle different file types. This included using PyMuPDF for Portable Document
Format (PDF) text extraction and Microsoftãs MarkItDown for converting formats such as
Word, Excel, and PowerPoint into clean Markdown [77, 78]. This multiôÙtool approach enôÙ
sured consistent, machineôÙreadable outputs across formats, allowing for reliable downôÙ
stream processing in the pipeline.
2. Extraction and Classification
With the cleaned text prepared, the next phase applied the optimized Phi-4 LLM and
its corresponding DSPy prompt programs. The relevant DSPy Signature for this task
is defined in Appendix Listing C.3. Since SharePoint documents are generally short,
each document was processed as a single input unit. The extraction strategy for these
internal documents was deliberately limited based on expert guidance. The consensus
was to prioritize the mining of historical deviation records, as they contain more valuable
fieldôÙtested knowledge. Consequently, to ensure the extraction from internal documents
remained focused on only the most foundational standards, the model was instructed to
extract a maximum of two critical CTs per document. To ensure consistency and quality,
the model was guided by the streamlined validation criteria defined in Table 3.5. Each valid
CT was then assigned to one of ten semantic categories from the taxonomy in Table 3.4.
3. CT Candidates
Following the extraction and classification process, 190 unique CTs were derived from
the 143 SharePoint pages and associated documents. This relatively low number, comôÙ
pared to the historical deviation dataset, reflects the strict extraction criteria: a maximum
of two concise CTs per document, each explicitly stated, nonôÙnegotiable, and directly
verifiable. Many documents did not meet these requirements, ensuring only highôÙquality
entries were included. These formalized CTs complement those mined from historical
deviations, together forming a comprehensive and reliable knowledge base.
4.3.3
Consolidated Company Truth Knowledge Base
The consolidated knowledge base comprises 2,532 canonical CTs, integrating insights
from both historical deviations and formal documentation. Of these, 2,342 originated from
deviation records, where expert commentary was systematically processed through exôÙ
traction, clustering, and synthesis to capture recurring practices and commonly observed
nonôÙconformities. An additional 190 were extracted from SharePointôÙbased internal docôÙ
uments, reflecting formally approved technical guidelines and organizational standards.
By combining these two sources, the knowledge base captures both the ãspiritã of engiôÙ
neering practice and the ãletterã of official policy. It now serves as a comprehensive and
traceable reference for the ALDEVI system. Building on this foundation, the next phase
focuses on preparing the CTs and customer requirements for efficient retrieval. This inôÙ
volves fineôÙtuning the embedding models to reflect better domainôÙspecific semantics and
selecting optimal chunking strategies for integration into the RAG pipeline.
4.4
Embedding Model Tuning
The accuracy and relevance of information retrieval in a RAG system rely heavily on
the quality of its embedding model. While all-mpnet-base-v2 showed strong genôÙ
eral performance and was practical to deploy during clustering, a key challenge remains:
the ãdomainôÙspecificity gap.ã Most offôÙtheôÙshelf models are trained on generic internet
data and struggle to capture the specialized terminology, abbreviations, and contextual
nuances found in industrial engineering and contractual texts. For example, a generalôÙ
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
41
==PAGE:56==
purpose model might treat ãAlfa Laval decanter specificationsã and ãdecanter specificaôÙ
tionsã as unrelated, missing a clear semantic connection. Such limitations directly impact
retrieval accuracy in domainôÙspecific settings.
To overcome the domainôÙspecificity gap and adapt all-mpnet-base-v2 to the unique
language of Alfa Lavalãs domain, the model underwent specialized tuning. In this context,
the following section details the process of generating a synthetic dataset and evaluating
two adaptation strategies: a lightweight linear adapter and direct fineôÙtuning.
4.4.1
Synthetic Dataset Generation for Domain Adaptation
TaskôÙspecific domain adaptation, particularly through fineôÙtuning, requires a substantial
amount of highôÙquality, domainôÙrelevant training data. As no large, labeled dataset of
questionãanswer pairs existed for this use case, a custom pipeline was created to genôÙ
erate synthetic data. It used internal Operator and Installation Manuals for Alfa Laval
decanters as source material, ensuring strong alignment with the target domain. The full
process is shown in Figure 4.6.
Raw
Manuals
Text
Chunking
Documents
Processing
Q&A

Processing
Figure 4.6: Synthetic dataset generation.
1. Raw Manuals
The Operator and Installation Manuals for Alfa Laval decanters, described in Section 3.3.2,
served as the primary source for generating the dataset. These PDF documents contain
authoritative, domainôÙspecific language, technical terminology, and detailed procedures
that the embedding model must learn to understand and represent effectively.
2. Document Processing
After collecting the manuals, the next step focused on converting the PDF files into clean,
extractable text. The PyMuPDF library was used to perform this transformation, following
the same methodology described in Section 4.3.2. Through this conversion, the unstrucôÙ
tured layout of the documents was transformed into plain text, making them suitable for
subsequent analysis and segmentation.
3. Text Chunking
Once the raw text had been extracted, the content was segmented into smaller, semanôÙ
tically meaningful units known as chunks. A RecursiveChunker was applied for this task,
configured with a target chunk size of approximately 800 tokens and an overlap of 400
tokens to maintain continuity. Token lengths were calculated using the tiktoken library
[79]. The recursive chunking method was chosen to preserve complete ideas within each
segment, resulting in coherent content blocks suitable for use as positive examples in
the upcoming training stage. This process yielded a corpus of 87 distinct chunks. NoôÙ
tably, the chunking configuration closely aligns with the default settings used by Azureãs
AI Foundry Agent Service file search tool, which applies the same parameters [80]. A
broader, formal evaluation of chunking strategies for the final RAG system is planned and
presented in Section 4.5.
4. LLM Processing
The generative phase of the pipeline centers on the use of the local Phi-4 LLM, which was
previously selected and optimized. The model processes each chunk using two structured
DSPy Signatures that support targeted content generation.
1. Topic Extraction: The first step involves the application of the ExtractTopicsFromôÙ
Chunk Signature, described in Appendix Listing C.5. The LLM identifies and exôÙ
tracts three specific topics, facts, or concepts from each chunk. The focus is on
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
42
==PAGE:57==
isolating minimal, selfôÙcontained ideas, which ensures that subsequent outputs reôÙ
main precise and grounded in the source material.
2. Question Generation: Following topic extraction, the model applies the ChunkQuesôÙ
tions Signature, outlined in Appendix Listing C.6. Based on the identified topics, the
model generates 20 naturalôÙlanguage questions that resemble user queries. To
better reflect authentic usage patterns, the questions are deliberately varied in tone
and structure, occasionally including informal phrasing or minor typographical erôÙ
rors. This variation is designed to replicate the way real users interact with search
systems while maintaining a clear semantic connection to each topic and its correôÙ
sponding source chunk.
5. Q&A Pairs and Dataset Finalization
The final step in the synthetic data pipeline focused on organizing the generated content
into structured questionãanswer pairs for embedding model training. The 87 text chunks
from the chunking stage formed the document corpus. For each chunk, the LLM produced
relevant questions, which were then paired with their source chunk to create positive
examples that capture clear queryãdocument relevance for supervised learning.
The resulting dataset comprised 87 unique chunks and 4,698 questionãchunk pairs. This
total was slightly below the expected 5,220 pairs, calculated based on three topics and 20
questions per chunk. The shortfall was due to occasional undergeneration by the quanôÙ
tized LLMs, which sometimes produced fewer items than specified by the DSPy prompts.
For training, the dataset was split into 3,758 pairs (80%) and 940 (20%) for validation.
With this dataset in place, the groundwork was laid for evaluating the base embedding
model and applying adaptation strategies such as linear adapters and full fineôÙtuning, as
described in the following sections.
4.4.2
Embedding Model Adaptation Strategies and Training
With the synthetic dataset in place, two adaptation strategies were explored to improve
the performance of the base all-mpnet-base-v2 model: direct fineôÙtuning and the use
of lightweight linear adapter layers. Both methods aim to enhance the modelãs ability to
understand Alfa Lavalãs technical and contractual language, helping to close the domainôÙ
specificity gap and improve retrieval accuracy within the ALDEVI system.
Base Model Benchmarking
Before any domainôÙspecific adaptation, the inherent information retrieval capabilities of
the base all-mpnet-base-v2 model were rigorously benchmarked on the generated
synthetic validation set. This step established a clear baseline for its performance within
the target domain. The evaluation used the InformationRetrievalEvaluator and SequenôÙ
tialEvaluator components from the sentenceôÙtransformers library, supporting Matryoshka
style truncation to assess performance across multiple embedding dimensions (768, 512,
256, 128, 64, and 32 dimensions) [81]. Standard Information Retrieval (IR) metrics, as inôÙ
troduced in Section 2.6.2, were employed, including NDCG@10, MRR@10, MAP@100,
as well as various accuracy@k, precision@k, and recall@k scores. The detailed raw
results for the base model across these dimensions are presented in Appendix Table E.1.
Linear Adapter Strategy
Linear adapters are a lightweight adaptation strategy that introduces a small, trainable
torch.nn.Linear layer applied only to the query embeddings before they are compared
to the document embeddings. This approach keeps the large, preôÙtrained base embedôÙ
ding model frozen, significantly reducing the number of trainable parameters and compuôÙ
tational requirements compared to full fineôÙtuning, while still allowing for some domainôÙ
specific alignment. A visual representation of this strategy is provided in Figure 4.7.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG

==PAGE:58==

Figure 4.7: Linear adapter strategy to improve embedding models.
Since each Matryoshka dimension required its own linear adapter, the training had to be
conducted sequentially, one dimension at a time. This contrasts with the joint optimization
used in full fineôÙtuning, resulting in a longer total training duration. The training itself relied
on TripletLoss, a standard metricôÙlearning objective that adjusts the adapterãs weights to
pull a query embedding (the anchor) closer to its correct document chunk (the positive
example) while pushing it away from an incorrect one (the negative example). To proôÙ
vide a strong contrastive signal, these negative examples were drawn from a completely
outôÙofôÙdomain source, the 2024 WHO Report. This technique is particularly effective for
small, specialized layers, such as adapters, as it forces the model to learn domainôÙspecific
features and align the query embeddings accordingly, all without altering the frozen base
model [82, 83]. Each adapter was trained for twenty epochs on the synthetic dataset,
at which point performance metrics such as recall and MRR plateaued, indicating conôÙ
vergence without overfitting. The detailed results for this adapterôÙenhanced model are
presented in Appendix Table E.2.
Direct Fine-Tuning Strategy
As a direct alternative to linear adapters, a fineôÙtuning strategy was implemented. This apôÙ
proach involves updating all parameters of the preôÙtrained all-mpnet-base-v2 model
using the domainôÙspecific synthetic dataset. Once fineôÙtuned, the resulting model is reôÙ
ferred to as AL-MPNet, representing its adaptation to Alfa Lavalãs technical and conôÙ
tractual language.
By modifying the entire model architecture, fineôÙtuning allows the
embedding model to learn new, nuanced internal representations tailored specifically to
Alfa Lavalãs technical and contractual language, thereby becoming deeply knowledgeable
about the target domainãs semantic relationships. A visual representation of this strategy
is provided in Figure 4.8.

Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
44
==PAGE:59==
The fineôÙtuning process employed Multiple Negative Ranking (MNR) for training. By treatôÙ
ing every other sample in the batch as a negative example, MNR captures diverse semanôÙ
tic distinctions across contexts. In contrast to TripletLoss, which considers one negative
at a time, MNR simultaneously pushes each anchor away from multiple inôÙbatch negôÙ
atives. Consequently, it is wellôÙsuited to broad model adaptation. When coupled with
Matryoshka, it enables the simultaneous optimization of all embedding subôÙdimensions
within a single training run. This made fineôÙtuning faster and more efficient than trainôÙ
ing separate linear adapters, while still producing embeddings that perform well even at
reduced dimensions.
FineôÙtuning was conducted for ten epochs on the synthetic dataset using MNR as the
loss function. Training employed a global batch size of 128, achieved by combining a
perôÙdevice batch size of 8 with sixteen gradient accumulation steps. A cosine learning
rate schedule with warmôÙup was applied, starting from a base rate of 2 û 10ã5. The
process was executed on consumerôÙgrade hardware (MacBook Air M3) and completed in
approximately two hours. Final evaluation results for the fineôÙtuned model are provided
in Appendix Table E.3.

was measured on the synthetic validation set. Table 4.5 summarizes the key retrieval
metrics at the 768ôÙdimensional embedding level, which corresponds to the modelãs full

In addition to the aggregated metrics, a representative example was examined to further

cosine similarities with both a relevant and an irrelevant document chunk, we observed

ãÂ The Base model yielded a similarity score of 0.43 for the relevant chunk and 0.17

This illustrative example demonstrates that the fineôÙtuned model more effectively distinôÙ

The fineôÙtuned AL-MPNet modelãs superior performance on domainôÙspecific data makes
it the strongest candidate for the embedding backbone of the ALDEVI system. Its ability
to create domainôÙaware representations is critical for enhancing downstream retrieval acôÙ
curacy. To finalize the retrieval stack, the following section evaluates how this promising
model interacts with various document segmentation strategies.
4.5
Chunking Strategy
With a domainôÙtuned embedding model in place, the next critical component in the reôÙ
trieval stack is how source documents are segmented. If a relevant clause is split across
two chunks or buried inside an overly long block, the retriever will not surface it, regardôÙ
less of the embedding quality. Because the business cost of missing a deviation is higher
than that of flagging a false positive, the primary focus of chunking needs to be recall.
4.5.1
Evaluation Framework and Methodology
To ensure a robust comparison, the evaluation was conducted using the chunkingôÙevaluation
library, an openôÙsource toolkit developed by the Chroma DB [59]. This framework proôÙ
vides a standardized method for benchmarking chunking and embedding combinations
across both generalôÙpurpose and domainôÙspecific datasets.
An exhaustive sweep was conducted to evaluate various chunking philosophies, which
were introduced in Section 2.4. The candidate strategies selected for this experiment
included a baseline FixedôÙSize Chunker, the more advanced RecursiveChunker, and
two distinct implementations of semantic chunking: the KamradtModifiedChunker, which
groups sentences based on local embedding similarity, and the ClusterSemanticChunker,
which uses a global clustering approach to form coherent groups. Notably, LLMôÙbased
chunking strategies were deliberately excluded from this evaluation. While potentially
powerful, their high computational cost and significant latency make them impractical for
production environments that require the efficient processing of large volumes of docuôÙ
ments.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
46
==PAGE:61==
Each chunking strategy was tested with a range of chunk sizes and overlaps (where
applicable) and paired with four distinct embedding models to assess their interaction.
These included: allôÙMiniLMôÙL6ôÙv2, allôÙmpnetôÙbaseôÙv2, multilingualôÙe5ôÙlargeôÙinstruct, and
the custom ALôÙMPNet model, which was fineôÙtuned on Alfa Laval domain data as detailed
in Section 4.4.
The evaluation itself was structured in two distinct phases to measure both general and
specialized performance. First, a General Evaluation assessed performance on a stanôÙ
dardized, generalôÙpurpose benchmark to establish a baseline for each chunker/embedôÙ
der combination. Second, a DomainôÙSpecific Evaluation applied the same evaluation
sweep to Alfa Lavalãs technical documents (Operator and Installation Manuals), for which
a set of queries and groundôÙtruth excerpts was synthetically generated using Phi-4 to
simulate realôÙworld use cases. While conceptually aligned with the pipeline used for fineôÙ
tuning data generation, this procedure was fully automated by the chunkingôÙevaluation
library, which required only the source documents as input to create precise pairs.
For every chunker and embedding combination, performance was measured against a set
of key metrics grounded in the standard IR principles outlined in Section 2.6.2. SpecifiôÙ
cally, four primary metrics provide a holistic view of performance:
ãÂ Recall: The proportion of relevant items successfully retrieved. It was regarded
as the most critical metric since failing to identify a deviation carries a significant
business cost.
ãÂ Precision: The proportion of retrieved items that were relevant, measuring the
signalôÙtoôÙnoise ratio of the retrieval.
ãÂ IoU: A measure of how well a retrieved chunk overlaps with the groundôÙtruth answer
span. Higher scores indicate less extraneous content and greater conciseness.
ãÂ Precision ãÎ: A stricter variant of precision, that specifically penalizes the retrieval
of chunks significantly larger than the target answer.
Together, these metrics enable a comprehensive analysis, allowing for the evaluation of
the critical tradeôÙoff between maximizing recall and retrieving precise, concise chunks.
4.5.2
Results and Analysis
The automated evaluation pipeline produced a comprehensive set of results, revealing
clear patterns in the performance of different chunking and embedding combinations.
The analysis confirmed that chunker performance is highly dependent on the embedôÙ
ding model used, underscoring the importance of optimizing these components in tandem
rather than in isolation.
General Evaluation
The first phase established a performance baseline on a generalôÙpurpose benchmark,
with full results available in Appendix Table F.1. While the absolute highest recall score
(0.878) was achieved by the FixedTokenChunker (800/400) paired with the powerful e5ôÙ
largeôÙinstruct model, this came at the cost of lower chunk quality. For instance, its IoU
score was only 0.014.
In contrast, the custom AL-MPNet model demonstrated a more balanced profile. When
paired with the RecursiveChunker (800/400), it achieved a strong recall of 0.832 while
delivering a higher IoU of 0.015 and a significantly better Precision ãÎof 0.067 compared
to the top recallerãs 0.046. This was a noteworthy result, demonstrating that the domainôÙ
tuned model could generalize effectively and produce higherôÙquality retrievals even on
nonôÙdomainôÙspecific text.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
47
==PAGE:62==
Domain-Specific Evaluation
In this critical phase, the domainôÙtuned modelãs advantage became definitive. The results
for the domainôÙspecific evaluation, detailed in Appendix Table F.2, highlight a crucial tradeôÙ
off between chunk conciseness and contextual integrity.
The RecursiveChunker (800/400) paired with AL-MPNet emerged as the undisputed reôÙ
call champion, achieving the highest score of 0.905 across all 56 combinations. However,
it is crucial to analyze this choice in the context of other metrics. For example, the KamôÙ
radtModifiedChunker (200) with the same model achieved a much higher IoU (0.053) and
Precision ãÎ(0.175), but at the cost of a significantly lower recall (0.833). This illustrates a
classic tradeôÙoff: smaller, more semantic chunks are naturally more precise but increase
the risk of missing information. The RecursiveChunker (800/400) configuration, with its
large chunk size and substantial overlap, prioritizes capturing the complete context of a reôÙ
quirement, even if it includes more surrounding text. In the context of deviation detection,
this is a favorable tradeôÙoff, as splitting a relevant clause could cause the downstream
LLM to misinterpret its meaning.
4.5.3
Final Selection
Based on the analysis of the evaluation results, the optimal configuration is unequivocally
the combination of the AL-MPNet embedding model and the RecursiveChunker with a
chunk size of 800 and an overlap of 400. This decision is based on the following key
findings:
1. Highest DomainôÙSpecific Recall: This combination delivered the highest recall
score (0.905) on the most relevant dataset, directly addressing the primary business
requirement of minimizing the risk of missed deviations.
2. Prioritization of Contextual Integrity: The choice deliberately prioritizes contexôÙ
tual completeness over chunkôÙlevel precision. While other configurations yielded
higher IoU scores, they did so with smaller chunks that risk fragmenting complex
technical clauses, which is unacceptable for the downstream reasoning task.
3. Robustness and Generalization: Strong, balanced results on the general dataset
show that domain tuning produced a model that performs well on specialized tasks
without losing general language understanding.
4. Maximized Semantic Continuity: The recursive chunking strategy, combined with
a considerable 400ôÙtoken overlap, is critical for retrieving complete clauses and reôÙ
quirements that might otherwise be arbitrarily split and lost, ensuring the LLM reôÙ
ceives unbroken context for its analysis.
With the textôÙbased retrieval components now optimized, the focus shifts to engineering
a complementary element for visual data. Accordingly, the following section details the
tuning of a vision embedding model to handle the technical diagrams and schematics
present in the source documents.
4.6
Vision Embedding Model Tuning
To support retrieval over technical diagrams and schematics, two multimodal strategies
were considered for the final system. The first relies on a dedicated vision embedding
model to encode images for retrieval. The second, exemplified by architectures like ColôÙ
Pali, processes raw image and text inputs directly.
Accordingly, this section details the engineering of the component for the first strategy:
a customôÙtuned vision embedding model named AL-CLIP, based on clip-ViT-L-14.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
48
==PAGE:63==
The goal is not to assume the superiority of a multimodal system, but to develop a comôÙ
petitive visual retriever for later comparison. FineôÙtuning of the ColPaLIôÙstyle architecture
was not pursued due to its significantly higher computational cost and architectural comôÙ
plexity. Its performance will instead be assessed using the preôÙtrained variant. Final
evaluation results, including comparisons with AL-CLIP, ColPali, and textôÙonly systems,
are presented in Section 5.4.
4.6.1
Synthetic Dataset Generation for Vision
The generation of a synthetic vision dataset followed a similar methodology to the one
used for text embeddings, as detailed in Section 4.4.1. The core principle was to use a
generative model to create a highôÙquality, domainôÙspecific dataset for contrastive learning,
though the implementation was adapted for the visual modality.
First, the pipeline programmatically extracted all embedded images from the source PDF
manuals using the PyMuPDF library [77]. After deduplicating the extracted images, a
VisionôÙLanguage Model (VLM), Gemma-3-27B-Q4_0 (whose selection is justified in SecôÙ
tion 5.3), was employed via Ollama to generate descriptive annotations.
A standard
prompting approach was necessary due to framework limitations at the time of experiôÙ
mentation, specifically the lack of direct image input support in DSPy and the early stage
of multimodal inference in the llama.cpp ecosystem, as mentioned in Section 2.2.1. ConôÙ
sequently, the VLM was prompted with two distinct instructions:
1. Positive Descriptions: To generate multiple, varied positive descriptions that acôÙ
curately describe the image content, mimicking how different users might refer to
the same diagram.
2. Negative Descriptions: To generate descriptions of concepts completely unrelated
to the image, providing strong negative examples for the training process.
The full prompts used for this generation process are detailed in Appendix G. The pipeline
processed an initial set of 303 unique images, ultimately yielding 1,509 records. This
was below the theoretical maximum of 3,030, due to two primary factors: inconsistent
outputs from the quantized VLM and processing failures on a small number of lowôÙquality
images. This shortfall mirrors the undergeneration observed in the textôÙbased pipeline
(Section 4.4.1). Finally, the dataset was split into training and test sets by unique image
ID, resulting in 1,208 records (from 242 images) for training and 301 records (from 61
images) for testing.
4.6.2
Model Adaptation and Training
The adaptation process began with the preôÙtrained clip-ViT-L-14 model, which was
first benchmarked on the heldôÙout test set and then fineôÙtuned on the synthetic training
data. To maintain methodological consistency with the text embedding experiments, the
MNR loss was used during training. Despite the dataset containing explicit negative pairs,
MNR was preferred for its ability to exploit inôÙbatch negatives, encouraging finerôÙgrained
distinction learning than fixed negative examples typically allow.
This contrastive approach aligns well with the original training paradigm of the CLIP arôÙ
chitecture, which also relied on largeôÙbatch contrastive learning. The loss was wrapped
in a MatryoshkaLoss to enable simultaneous optimization across all embedding subôÙ
dimensions. Key training parameters were matched to those used in the text model to
ensure experimental comparability.
4.6.3
Performance Evaluation and Final Selection
The fineôÙtuned model, designated AL-CLIP, was evaluated against the base model on
the heldôÙout test set. The full Matryoshka evaluation results for both models are detailed
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
49
==PAGE:64==
in Appendix H. A direct comparison of their performance at the whole 768ôÙdimensional
embedding level is summarized in Table 4.6.

The evaluation results confirm that fineôÙtuning yielded substantial and consistent perforôÙ

much more effectively than the base model.
Although the absolute performance scores are lower than those achieved in the textôÙbased
experiments, which reflects the inherent difficulty of the imageôÙtoôÙtext retrieval task, the
relative gains from fineôÙtuning remain substantial. The process successfully adapted the
model to the specific visual language of Alfa Lavalãs technical documentation. Given its
enhanced performance, AL-CLIP is confirmed as a properly optimized component and is
selected as the dedicated vision embedding model for the multimodal architecture to be
evaluated in Section 5.4. With AL-CLIP in place, the full suite of AI components across
both text and vision is now complete. The final step is to select an appropriate vector
database to store and efficiently query these embeddings.
4.7
The Vector Database
A specialized vector database is required to support efficient, lowôÙlatency similarity searches
over the millions of embeddings produced by the system. To identify the best option, a
comparative analysis of six leading solutions was conducted, guided by the projectãs core
requirements for a selfôÙhosted, scalable, and enterpriseôÙready platform.
4.7.1
Candidate Landscape and Evaluation Criteria
The candidates evaluated were Chroma DB [84], Milvus [85], Pinecone [86], PgVector
[87], Qdrant [88], and Weaviate [89]. Each was assessed across several critical axes:
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
50
==PAGE:65==
ãÂ Deployment Model & Licensing: The ability to selfôÙhost under an openôÙsource
license is a nonôÙnegotiable requirement for data sovereignty.
ãÂ Core Architecture & Scalability: The underlying architectureãs ability to handle
datasets larger than available Random Access Memory (RAM) and scale efficiently
on commodity hardware.
ãÂ Performance & RAGôÙSpecific Features: Support for fast metadata filtering and
advanced retrieval strategies, such as hybrid search, which are critical for the planned
systemôÙlevel evaluations.

4.7.2
Comparative Analysis and Final Selection
The evaluation began by filtering the candidates against the projectãs strictest constraints.
As a cloudôÙonly SaaS product, Pinecone was immediately disqualified due to the nonôÙ
negotiable requirement for a selfôÙhosted solution that guarantees data sovereignty. The
remaining openôÙsource candidates were then assessed for their architectural fit. PgVecôÙ
tor, while an excellent solution for adding vector capabilities to an existing PostgreSQL
environment, was set aside in favor of a purposeôÙbuilt, standalone vector search engine to
avoid potential performance contention. Similarly, Milvus, with its complex, microservicesôÙ
based architecture, is designed for massive, multiôÙbillion vector deployments. Its operaôÙ
tional overhead was deemed excessive for the projectãs singleôÙserver prototype needs.
The final decision came down to a comparison between Chroma DB, Weaviate, and
Qdrant.
ãÂ Chroma DB is exceptionally easy to use for prototyping, but its inôÙmemoryôÙfirst arôÙ
chitecture poses a significant scalability risk for an enterprise system. A dataset
exceeding available RAM could lead to performance degradation or failure.
ãÂ Weaviate offers a powerful, ãbatteriesôÙincludedã system with builtôÙin hybrid search
and vectorization. However, the project required a more modular setup, focused
solely on storage and retrieval, which enabled the development and testing of cusôÙ
tom hybrid search logic within the application layer.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
51
==PAGE:66==
ãÂ Qdrant emerged as the ideal choice, perfectly aligning with all project requirements.
It directly addresses the scalability concern with its onôÙdisk storage and memory
mapping architecture, ensuring it can handle datasets far larger than system RAM
on commodity hardware. Its implementation in Rust and highly optimized Hierarchical
Navigable Small World (HNSW) index guarantees lowôÙlatency queries. Crucially, its
advanced and swift metadata filtering is a prerequisite for the custom hybrid search
architectures planned for evaluation in the next chapter.
Based on its superior onôÙdisk scalability, highôÙperformance filtering capabilities, and modôÙ
ular design, which is suitable for advanced RAG experimentation, Qdrant was selected
as the vector database component for the ALDEVI system.
4.8
Key Takeaways and Selected Toolkit
This chapter details the systematic, experimentôÙdriven process of selecting and optimizing
each component of the ALDEVI system. From the generative engine to the data persisôÙ
tence layer, each decision was guided by quantitative benchmarks and qualitative expert
validation. With all components now selected and validated, all requirements for Gate 2
ã Component Engineering have been fulfilled.
The complete toolkit of optimized components selected in this chapter includes:
ãÂ Generation Engine: Phi-4-Q4_K_M ã Chosen for its superior balance of reasonôÙ
ing, factual accuracy, and qualitative preference from domain experts.
ãÂ Prompt Program: DSPy with KôÙNearest Neighbors (KNN) and Labeled FewôÙShot
optimizers ã Selected for delivering a programmatic performance lift of over 20%
compared to a baseline approach.
ãÂ Knowledge Base: A curated set of 2,532 canonical CTs ã Engineered by inteôÙ
grating 2,342 truths mined from fieldôÙtested historical deviations with 190 truths exôÙ
tracted from codified internal standards, creating a comprehensive and verifiable
source of truth.
ãÂ Text Embedding Model: FineôÙtuned AL-MPNet ã Justified by an 82% improvement
in NDCG score on domainôÙspecific data over the base model.
ãÂ Vision Embedding Model: FineôÙtuned AL-CLIP ã Selected after demonstrating a
90% improvement in NDCG on the domainôÙspecific imageôÙtext retrieval task.
ãÂ Chunking Strategy: RecursiveChunker (800/400) ã Chosen for achieving the highôÙ
est recall (0.905) on domain data while preserving the contextual integrity necessary
for downstream reasoning.
ãÂ Vector Database: Qdrant ã Selected for its highôÙperformance, onôÙdisk storage arôÙ
chitecture, which is essential for local deployment and scalability.
While each component has been individually optimized, its actual effectiveness is only
revealed when it operates with the rest of the system. The research now proceeds to
Gate 3 ã System Architecture Selection. The next chapter, therefore, transitions from
componentôÙlevel engineering to endôÙtoôÙend system evaluation, where these optimized
parts will be integrated into complete RAG pipelines and benchmarked against each other
to select the final, most effective system architecture.

5
System Integration and Performance
Evaluation
5.1
End-to-End Evaluation Protocol
To systematically compare the candidate architectures, a comprehensive evaluation proôÙ
tocol was designed. The protocol defines the hardware environment, the test dataset,
the evaluation procedure, and the key performance metrics that determine the optimal
system.
5.1.1
Experimental Environment and Tooling
While most of the component engineering was conducted on consumerôÙgrade hardware to
ensure feasibility, the vision model tuning detailed in Section 4.6 required a more powerful
environment. The same dedicated private multiôÙGPU workstation was used for all endôÙtoôÙ
end evaluations in this chapter, running Ubuntu 22.04 LTS with 64 GB of system RAM. The
system is equipped with three NVIDIA RTX 3060 Ti cards (Ampere, 8 GB GDDR6 each)
and one GTX 1080 (Pascal, 8 GB GDDR5X), delivering a theoretical peak performance
of approximately 57.6 Tera FloatingôÙpoint Operations Per Seconds (TFLOPs), more than
a 14ôÙfold increase over the baseline MacBook Air (M3, 16 GB unified memory), which
provides roughly 4.1 TFLOPs. Furthermore, a custom Python wrapper, dspy_llamacpp
[90], developed for this project, integrates the llama.cpp server with the DSPy framework,
streamlining server orchestration and GPU selection during evaluation.
To illustrate the practical performance difference, a single reasoningôÙheavy query was
executed on both systems. The MacBook Air averaged 8.6 Tokens per Second (TPS).
Meanwhile, the multiôÙGPU workstation achieved 31.7 TPS, resulting in a nearly 3.7x
speedup in practical inference throughput. Given this substantial advantage, all subseôÙ
quent evaluations were conducted on the workstation.
Moreover, secure remote access to the server was established using a private encrypted
network built with WireGuard for traffic routing and Tailscale for authentication, as illusôÙ
trated in Figure 5.1 [91]. Such a configuration enabled secure development via Secure
Shell Protocol (SSH) with public key authentication, all while avoiding exposure to public
IP addresses.
Coordination
Server
Device 1
Mesh VPN
public keys and metadata
Device N
Server
Figure 5.1: Secure remote access architecture using Tailscale and WireGuard.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
53
==PAGE:68==
The architecture, as shown, uses Tailscale to establish a secure mesh network based
on the zeroôÙtrust principle. The coordination server operated by Tailscale functions as
the control plane, managing device authentication and facilitating the exchange of public
keys. It does not route or access data traffic, ensuring that private keys remain confined
to each device. Once authenticated, nodes form direct, peerôÙtoôÙpeer connections over a
private network known as a ãTailnet.ã These connections make up the data plane, where
all traffic is encrypted endôÙtoôÙend using the WireGuard protocol [91, 92]. The resulting
setup effectively creates a secure, virtual Local Area Network (LAN), enabling fast and
private communication without exposing the server to the public internet or relying on a
central routing point.
5.1.2
Evaluation Dataset and Query Generation
The evaluation protocol revolves around the structured historical deviation dataset cuôÙ
rated in Section 3.2. To create a focused yet challenging test set, domain experts at Alfa
Laval selected five projects from the corpus, chosen for their complexity and representaôÙ
tiveness, and also because this scale still lets the full benchmark run overnight on a single
workstation, enabling rapid iteration during system evaluation. For each project, the entire
set of customer documents serves as the search corpus against which the RAG system
is tested, with each project containing roughly 20 files that expand to about 1,000 vecôÙ
torized text chunks after 800/400ôÙtoken segmentation. Table 5.1 summarizes how many
deviation groups and CT queries each project contributes.

The central challenge in designing the evaluation lies in the relationship between the
groundôÙtruth data and the queries used by the RAG system. The ground truth for a deôÙ
viation corresponds to the expertãs al_comments field, which links to a single customer
requirement. However, as detailed in the knowledgeôÙmining pipeline (Section 4.3), a sinôÙ
gle, rich expert comment can be deconstructed into multiple, distinct CTs. These exôÙ
tracted CTs then serve as the queries for the system. A concrete example clarifies the
oneôÙtoôÙmany mapping:
ãÂ Customer Requirement: ãAll external surfaces must be painted with a threeôÙcoat
epoxy system.ã
ãÂ Expert Comment (Ground Truth): ãThis is a deviation. Our standard is a twoôÙ
coat system. We also do not use epoxy paint on the gearbox, and all fasteners are
supplied selfôÙcolored.ã
ãÂ Extracted CTs (System Queries):
1. ãAlfa Lavalãs standard coating is a twoôÙcoat system.ã
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
54
==PAGE:69==
2. ãThe gearbox is not painted with an epoxy system.ã
3. ãFasteners are supplied selfôÙcolored.ã
In the scenario above, a single deviation event generates three distinct queries for the
system to process. The customer document may contain text relevant to some, yet not
necessarily all, of the truths. For instance, the system might locate material on the twoôÙ
coat system but not on the fasteners. To accommodate this complexity, the evaluation
dataset was constructed using the following approach for each of the five projects:
ãÂ Positive Queries: The complete set of all CTs extracted from that projectãs historical
deviation logs is used as the positive queries.
ãÂ Negative Queries: A roughly equal number of ãhard negativeã queries are selected
from other projectsã CTs, chosen based on maximum cosine dissimilarity to ensure
they are highly unlikely to be relevant to the current project.
The resulting ãoneôÙtoôÙoneã matching of positive and negative cases creates a perfectly
balanced evaluation dataset. However, the oneôÙtoôÙmany mapping between a single expert
comment and its multiple queries means that a simple pass/fail judgment for each query is
insufficient. A more sophisticated procedure is therefore required to derive a final verdict
for each customer requirement, as detailed in the following section.
5.1.3
Evaluation Procedure and Metrics
To address the oneôÙtoôÙmany mapping between a single expert comment and its multiôÙ
ple CTs queries, a twoôÙstage evaluation procedure was implemented to derive a final,
scorable verdict for each customer requirement.
First, in the individual labeling stage, each CT query is processed independently by the
candidate RAG pipeline. The systemãs output for each query is a binary label: Deviation
or No Deviation. This evaluation focuses solely on the binary decision. The semantic
category of each CT (e.g., ãFinancial,ã ãTechnicalã) helps organize the knowledge base
but is not assessed for correctness, as there is no ground truth for it in the historical data.
Second, during the group aggregation stage, individual labels are organized by their
original groundôÙtruth identifier. For every group, the proportion of predictions that match
the correct class is calculated: Deviation in positive cases, and No Deviation in negative
cases. The resulting proportion is then compared to a predefined decision threshold to
determine whether the group has been correctly classified. In this setup, the threshold
defines the minimum fraction of correct individual labels required for the system to be
considered successful on that group. This approach ensures that evaluation accounts for
both false negatives in positive cases and false positives in negative ones. Five decision
thresholds were used to evaluate groupôÙlevel classification performance. Each defines the
minimum proportion of correct predictions required for a group to be considered correctly
classified: at least 1 (at least one correct prediction), ãË25%, ãË50%, ãË75%, and 100%.
For example, consider a group of five queries with a ground truth of Deviation, where
the system labels one as Deviation and the other four as No Deviation. With only 20%
correct predictions, the group is correctly classified under the ãat least 1ã threshold (a true
positive), but incorrectly classified as No Deviation under the ãË25%, ãË50%, ãË75%, and
100% thresholds (false negatives). Conversely, in a group of four queries with a ground
truth of No Deviation, if the system correctly labels one and incorrectly labels the other
three as Deviation (25% correct predictions), the group is correctly classified under the
ãat least 1ã and ãË25% thresholds (a true negative), but misclassified as Deviation under
the ãË50%, ãË75% and 100%thresholds (false positives).
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
55
==PAGE:70==
Based on the final classifications derived from this procedure, the overall effectiveness of
each candidate architecture is measured using the standard classification metrics introôÙ
duced in Section 2.6.3, including accuracy, precision, recall (TPR), F1 score, and False
Positive Rate (FPR). Throughout the analysis, recall is considered the most critical metôÙ
ric, as the business cost of failing to identify a true deviation is exceptionally high. While
all thresholds are analyzed, the results discussion will primarily focus on the ãat least 1ã
rule, which is the most sensitive to detecting any potential deviation signal.
5.1.4
Protocol Scope and Limitations
It is crucial to acknowledge that the evaluation protocol, although designed to be robust
for comparative analysis, possesses inherent limitations. The primary source of bias is
structural, as the evaluation procedure is not ãblindã. The scoring logic relies on prior
knowledge of whether a test case is a true positive or negative. This dependency, in
combination with the oneôÙtoôÙmany mapping from expert comments to CTs, needs the use
of a thresholding mechanism. This mechanism serves as a necessary heuristic rather
than a perfect representation of the decisionôÙmaking process. Finally, the ground truth
itself is subject to the limitations of human expertise, a concept known as interôÙrater reliaôÙ
bility, where experts can occasionally make errors or disagree on subjective cases. The
system is therefore measured against the best available benchmark, not a perfect one.
Therefore, the primary goal of the chapter is to identify the bestôÙperforming relative arôÙ
chitecture under controlled conditions. The results are intended to guide the selection
of the most promising pipeline rather than to serve as a definitive measure of absolute,
realôÙworld accuracy.
Finally, the proper and unbiased validation of the final systemãs
performance on entirely new projects, where the system must operate independently of
experimental scaffolds, is presented in Chapter 6.
5.2
Foundational Framework Validation
Before proceeding with advanced system engineering, it was critical to empirically valiôÙ
date the central hypothesis of this research: that the customôÙengineered CT framework
is superior to a traditional Formal Requirements approach for this specific industrial doôÙ
main. As argued in Section 3.4, the standard ãshallã statements were deemed too rigid
and contextôÙpoor. Therefore, the following section presents the results of a direct, headôÙ
toôÙhead comparison to test this claim.
To conduct this foundational test, a Baseline RAG Architecture was defined using the
optimal components selected in Chapter 4. The specific implementation for each stage
of this baseline pipeline is detailed in Table 5.2.

In the pipeline, customer tender documents are first parsed using a combination of fast
libraries to handle various file types, including PyMuPDF for PDFs and MarkItDown for
other office formats. After extraction, the text is segmented by the RecursiveChunker,
and each resulting chunk is converted into a vector by the fineôÙtuned AL-MPNet model
before being indexed in Qdrant. During retrieval, an input query (a CT) is embedded usôÙ
ing the same model, allowing Qdrant to perform a similarity search and identify the most
relevant document chunks. The retrieved chunks are then combined with the original
query into a structured prompt through a simple DSPy Signature called CheckTruth (ApôÙ
pendix Listing C.7). Following this, the augmented prompt is sent to the Phi-4 model,
which produces the final binary label: Deviation or No Deviation.
Two versions of this baseline system were then constructed.
The only variable that
changed between them was the knowledge base they queried: one system used formal
requirements, while the other used the CT framework. Both were evaluated on the same
fiveôÙproject test set using the evaluation protocol detailed previously. The results, sumôÙ
marized in Table 5.3, are decisive. The full performance breakdown across all decision
thresholds for both the Requirements and CTs framework is available in Appendix TaôÙ
ble I.1 and Table I.2, respectively.

The CTs framework dramatically outperformed the formal requirements framework, achievôÙ
ing an F1 score of 0.801 compared to just 0.509, representing a 29ôÙpoint improvement.
The underlying reason becomes clear when examining the tradeôÙoff between recall and
precision. Although the requirements framework achieved a slightly higher recall (0.818),
its FPR was extremely high at 0.749, which led to very poor precision (0.371). ConseôÙ
quently, the system was generating an unacceptably high number of false alarms, making
it impractical for realôÙworld use. In comparison, the CTs framework delivered a much more
balanced and helpful performance. It maintained a strong Recall (0.777) while achieving
a significantly lower FPR of just 0.233. As a result, it produced high precision (0.827) and
a much better overall accuracy (0.773).
The outcome empirically confirms that the flexibility and contextôÙawareness of the CTs
framework are essential for accurate deviation detection in this domain. With this foundaôÙ
tional choice validated, the Company Truths Baseline will now serve as the benchmark
against which all more advanced architectures are measured in the remainder of this
chapter.
5.3
Candidate Architectures
With the CTs baseline established and validated, the subsequent phase focused on deôÙ
signing more advanced architectures to investigate potential performance enhancements.
Each candidate was constructed to systematically evaluate the effects of varying comôÙ
ponents and strategies across the RAG pipeline. These experimental designs aimed to
leverage the strengths of the baseline while examining specific elements of retrieval, rankôÙ
ing, and generation.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG

==PAGE:72==
A key consideration for these advanced systems was the choice of the generative model.
Phiã4 had been thoroughly benchmarked and was the best performer on every textôÙonly
task. Yet once the experiments shifted to the multiôÙGPU workstation, larger models beôÙ
came practical for other roles. The multimodal variants needed native visionôÙlanguage
comprehension, which Phiã4 could not provide. An April 2025 leaderboard review idenôÙ
tified several quantised VLMs, but most exceeded the 32 GB VRAM ceiling or would
take too long to evaluate. Among the models that could run locally, Gemmaã3ã27BãQ4_0
topped public multimodal benchmarks [71, 93]. Because it balanced performance, VRAM

Gemmaã3ã27B for the multimodal ones. These choices define the nine candidate archiôÙ

This first group of architectures focuses on the generative component while maintaining

Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
58

the baseline was augmented with a DSPy CoT Module in order to evaluate the effects of
introducing a more sophisticated reasoning process. This module modifies the prompt so
that the model initially generates a stepôÙbyôÙstep rationale explaining its reasoning before
producing the final Deviation or No Deviation label.
Second, the impact of using a more powerful generator was examined through the inteôÙ
gration of the Gemma-3-27B model. Its inclusion was primarily motivated by the demands
of multimodal architectures, which require a generator capable of native visionôÙlanguage
understanding. Because this model was already being integrated, it was additionally evalôÙ
uated as a direct substitute for Phi-4 in both the standard and CoT textôÙonly pipelines,
allowing the effect of the model scale to be assessed independently.
5.3.2
Advanced Retrieval Architectures
The set of candidates evaluates the alternative hypothesis that improving the quality of
the retrieved context has a greater impact than enhancing the generator. Two distinct
advanced textôÙretrieval strategies were implemented.
The first approach is a Reranker Architecture. In this pipeline, the retrieval process is
modified into a twoôÙstage ãretrieveôÙthenôÙrerankã funnel. Under this setup, the initial dense
retriever (AL-MPNet) fetches a large set of 50 candidate chunks. A more computationally
intensive but accurate crossôÙencoder model (MiniLM-L6-v2) then reôÙevaluates these
50 candidates, reordering them to select the top 5 most relevant chunks to pass to the
generator while adhering to context window limits. This architecture was tested with both
Phi-4 and Gemma-3-27B as generators.
The second and most sophisticated approach is the Hybrid Architecture. The design of
this pipeline combines the strengths of three different retrieval paradigms: sparse, dense,
and lateôÙinteraction to maximize relevance. The process, illustrated in Figure 5.3, operôÙ
ates as follows:
Ingestion
Retrieval
Documents
Sparse Emb.
Dense Emb.
Vector DB
Sparse Emb.
Late Iter. Emb.
Sparse Emb.

Sparse Emb.
Dense Emb.
Hybrid Search
Sparse Emb.
Late Iter. Emb.
Sparse Emb.
Chunks
Top-k Reranked
Figure 5.3: The multiôÙstage process of the hybrid retrieval architecture.
At query time, the input CT is first encoded into three distinct representations: a dense
vector using AL-MPNet, a sparse vector representation derived from BM25, and a set
of tokenôÙlevel embeddings for late interaction using ColBERT-v2. The retrieval process
then continues in two main stages:
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
59
==PAGE:74==
1. Candidate Prefetch: The system performs a parallel search within Qdrant, using
both the dense and sparse query vectors to retrieve a combined set of candidate
chunks from their respective indexes. This step gathers a broad set of documents
that are relevant either semantically or lexically.
2. LateôÙInteraction Reranking: The combined set of candidates is then passed to the
ColBERT-v2 reranker. Rather than comparing single vectors, the lateôÙinteraction
model performs a fineôÙgrained, tokenôÙlevel similarity calculation by comparing the
queryãs token embeddings against the tokens of each candidate chunk. This apôÙ
proach enables a more precise reordering of the results.
The topôÙranked chunks, as scored by ColBERTôÙv2, are then used to form the final context
for the Phi-4 generator.
5.3.3
Multimodal Architectures
The final set of candidates tests the hypothesis that incorporating visual information from
the source documents can improve deviation detection. Two distinct multimodal paradigms
were implemented to explore this.
The first approach, Multimodal Retrieval, extends the dense baseline. It performs two
parallel searches across two separate collections in the vector database: one for text
chunks using AL-MPNet and one for images using the fineôÙtuned AL-CLIP model. The
topôÙranked text chunks and images are then combined and passed as context to the
multimodal Gemma-3-27B generator, which is capable of interpreting both modalities.
In contrast, the EndôÙtoôÙEnd Multimodal architecture uses a ColPaliôÙstyle model (ColQwen
-2.5-v0.2). As illustrated in Figure 5.4, the paradigm represents a significant architecôÙ
tural shift. During ingestion, the entire document is encoded as a sequence of image
patches by a vision encoder, and these patch embeddings are stored. At query time, the
text query is encoded into token embeddings. The system then performs a lateôÙinteraction
search, calculating the maximum similarity (MaxSim) between the query tokens and the
image patch embeddings to find the most relevant page regions [94]. In turn, this process
enables a deeper, crossôÙmodal analysis that would not be possible with separate retrieval
steps.

the results of their comparative evaluation, analyzing their effectiveness and efficiency (laôÙ

Analysis of Generator-Side Enhancements
The generatorôÙside enhancements yielded mixed results. Simply swapping to the larger
Gemma Generator provided a slight boost in recall over the baseline (0.811 vs. 0.777).
However, it came at the cost of a significantly higher FPR (0.350 vs. 0.233), which reôÙ
sulted in a lower F1 score. In other words, a more powerful generator, without improved
context, appears more prone to hallucination or overôÙinterpretation. In contrast, the CoT
prompting architecture represents the opposite extreme. It achieved nearôÙperfect preôÙ
cision (0.983) and a nearôÙzero FPR, yet suffered a catastrophic drop in recall to 0.399.
The model is overly cautious behavior, where it often fails to act, aligns with the ãIllusion
of Thinkingã phenomenon discussed in Section 2.5. This outcome suggests that imposôÙ
ing a complex reasoning process on a task that can be solved with highôÙquality context
may hinder performance. Notably, even pairing CoT with the larger Gemma model could
not fully overcome this recall deficit, confirming that the reasoning strategy itself, not the
model size, was the limiting factor.
Analysis of Advanced Retrieval Architectures
The advanced retrieval architectures confirmed that retrieval quality is the most critical
lever for this problem. The simple Reranking model provided a marginal but noticeable
improvement over the baseline, boosting the F1 score to 0.804. Nevertheless, the Hybrid
Retrieval architecture delivered the best overall performance. It achieved a strong recall
of 0.797 while maintaining a low FPR of 0.233. As a result, the combination yielded the
highest F1 score (0.814) and the best overall accuracy (0.785) of any candidate, demonôÙ
strating a superior balance of finding true deviations while minimizing false alarms.
Analysis of Multimodal Architectures
Finally, the multimodal architectures present a clear and cautionary tale. The Multimodal
Retrieval pipeline achieved the highest recall of any system (0.885), successfully identiôÙ
fying more true deviations than any other approach. Even so, the impressive sensitivity
came at a steep price, with an extremely high FPR (0.534). The Colpali architecture exôÙ
hibited a similar, though less pronounced, trend, also sacrificing higher recall for a higher
FPR (0.398). Such an outcome indicates that while the multimodal models are sensitive
to potential issues, they currently struggle to distinguish true visual signals from noise in
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
61
==PAGE:76==
the domain. Consequently, the models produce an unacceptable number of false alarms
for a practical business application.

( Table 5.1), a 40s perôÙquery latency would require ã8 h for a single full run.
The efficiency analysis further reinforces the conclusions drawn from the effectiveness
results. The Hybrid Retrieval architecture, although it employs a complex multiôÙstage
retrieval process, maintains a moderate total latency of approximately 12.2 seconds. Its
efficiency results from a wellôÙdesigned pipeline in which the parallel dense and sparse
searches operate extremely quickly. At the same time, the computationally expensive
ColBERTôÙv2 reranker is applied only to a small, preôÙfiltered set of candidates. As a result,
the system delivers highly accurate results with a delay that remains acceptable for an
interactive workflow.
In contrast, the architectures that showed promise in specific effectiveness metrics proved
to be prohibitively slow. The CoT pipelines incurred a significant latency penalty, with the
CoT + Gemma variant taking over 41 seconds. This is a direct result of the much longer
text generation required for the model to produce its stepôÙbyôÙstep reasoning before giving
a final answer. Similarly, both multimodal pipelines needed over 40 seconds per query.
The large multimodal generator drove the latency of the Multimodal Retrieval architecture,
while the Colpali system was additionally slowed by its computationally expensive process
of embedding entire pages as images. The oneôÙtime file preparation costs also reflect this
complexity, with multimodal systems requiring significantly more time to ingest documents
compared to textôÙonly extraction.
The combined effectiveness and efficiency data highlight clear tradeôÙoffs that directly imôÙ
pact the business use case. Architectures with the highest recall (Multimodal) or highest
precision (CoT) suffered from excessive latency, making them impractical for the fastôÙ
paced workflow of E&S engineers. On the other hand, the fastest models did not consisôÙ
tently deliver the accuracy needed for reliable deviation detection. These results demonôÙ
strate that selecting the optimal system is not merely about maximizing performance in
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
62
==PAGE:77==
one area, but about finding a balanced solution that addresses both technical and operôÙ
ational requirements. The optimal architecture is the one that delivers strong accuracy
without slowing down the review process.

racy and its speed. To make a definitive architectural choice, these competing factors
must be synthesized and balanced. The following figures serve this purpose, visually
plotting the performance of all nine candidates by synthesizing the detailed results from
Table 5.5 and Table 5.6.

The visualizations provide a straightforward narrative. The F1 Score vs. Latency plot
(Figure 5.5) reveals a significant performanceôÙcost imbalance for several architectures.
Models C, D, H, and I incur a substantial latency penalty, exceeding 30 seconds, withôÙ
out offering a commensurate improvement in F1 score over the faster candidates. This
makes their value proposition for an interactive workflow questionable. Among the viable,
lowôÙlatency candidates, the Hybrid Retrieval architecture (G) occupies the Pareto front,
offering the highest F1 score. The Precision vs. Recall plot (Figure 5.6) reinforces this
choice by highlighting the systemãs balance. It avoids the extremes of the overly cautious
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
63
==PAGE:78==
CoT Prompting (C) and the overly aggressive Multimodal Retrieval (H), positioning itself
in the desirable topôÙright quadrant of high precision and high recall. These visual analyses
lead to several key takeaways from the systemôÙlevel evaluation:
ãÂ Retrieval is the decisive factor: The results demonstrate that the quality and soôÙ
phistication of the retrieval pipeline are the most critical components for success.
The Hybrid architectureãs advanced, multiôÙfaceted retrieval outperformed all other
systems, including those with a larger generator, proving that providing superior
context is the primary driver of performance.
ãÂ Complex reasoning can be fragile: A complex CoT process proved counterproôÙ
ductive. It experienced a significant drop in recall, aligning with the ãIllusion of ThinkôÙ
ingã phenomenon [51], where models tend to falter under excessive cognitive load.
This fragility may be aggravated by quantization, suggesting that for this task, a
direct generation approach grounded in a highôÙquality context is more robust.
ãÂ Multimodal architectures show potential, but lack maturity: Multimodal sysôÙ
tems demonstrate significant potential, achieving the highest recall of any tested
architecture. However, this sensitivity currently comes at the cost of an unacceptôÙ
ably high FPR and prohibitive latency. Future advancements in model efficiency
and less aggressive quantization could make them powerful contenders, but they
are not yet practical for this productionôÙoriented use case.
Based on this analysis, the Hybrid Retrieval architecture is unequivocally selected as the
final system for the ALDEVI prototype. With this decision, all requirements for Gate 3 ã
System Architecture Selection have been fulfilled. The research now proceeds to the
final and most critical stage: Gate 4 ã Prototype Validation. The next chapter brings this
selected design to life, describing the implementation of the functional ALDEVI prototype
and presenting the results of its final validation.
