6
Implementation and Final Validation
6.1
ALDEVI System Implementation
The ALDEVI prototype is built on a modern, modular architecture designed for rapid deôÙ
velopment, local deployment, and data sovereignty1. The overall structure, depicted in
Figure 6.1, separates the userôÙfacing application from a set of orchestrated backend serôÙ
vices, ensuring a clear separation of concerns.
FastAPI
SQLite
Database
Main Server
Llama.cpp
Qdrant
Redis
Aux. Servers
Next.js
Frontend
Backend
Figure 6.1: ALDEVI system architecture diagram for local deployment.
The platformãs frontend is a dynamic web application built with Next.js [95], chosen for its
ability to create a fast and responsive user experience. This interface communicates with
a core backend server built with FastAPI [96], which provides a highôÙperformance API
and interfaces with a SQLite database [97] for managing nonôÙvector data such as user
information and project metadata.
The central server orchestrates three specialized auxiliary services to handle intensive
tasks. All generative AI tasks are managed by a dedicated inference server running the
Phi-4-Q4_K_M model, selected in Section 4.1, via the Llama.cpp engine, which was
identified as a premier tool for local inference in Section 2.2.1. The integration is enabled
by the custom dspy_llamacpp wrapper developed for this project, which allows the DSPy
prompting framework to communicate with Llama.cpp engine seamlessly [90]. A dediôÙ
cated Qdrant instance manages vector storage and similarity search, using the database
selected in Section 4.7 for its performance and onôÙdisk scalability. In addition, to enable
1Due to internal confidentiality constraints, this section omits screenshots, interface visuals, and source
code. Since ALDEVI is intended for production deployment at Alfa Laval, only highôÙlevel architectural and
functional details are presented.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
65
==PAGE:80==
a collaborative and realôÙtime user experience, a Redis server manages session state and
broadcasts updates to all connected clients.
To illustrate how these components interact, the process of creating and analyzing a new
project follows these sequential steps:
1. Project creation and upload: A user initiates the process by creating a project
and uploading customer documentation through the Next.js frontend. This action
triggers an API request to the FastAPI server.
2. Metadata and ingestion kickôÙoff: The FastAPI server first saves the projectãs
metadata (e.g., project name) to the SQLite database. It then immediately starts
the document ingestion pipeline as a background task, where the uploaded files are
parsed, segmented into chunks, and vectorized.
3. Indexing in vector store: The resulting document chunks and their corresponding
vectors are indexed into a new, projectôÙspecific collection within the Qdrant server,
making them ready for retrieval.
4. Notification and automated analysis trigger: Once ingestion is complete, the
server uses Redis to broadcast a realôÙtime status update to all connected clients,
notifying all connected clients that the project is ready for review. This event also
serves as a trigger, automatically kicking off the analysis phase.
5. Automated deviation detection: The server then systematically executes the HyôÙ
brid Retrieval pipeline for each verified CT. For each truth, it queries Qdrant, builds
a prompt, and sends an inference request to the Llama.cpp server. As the Phi-4
model generates results, they are saved to the database and displayed on the userãs
interface, allowing them to view the deviation analysis populated live without any furôÙ
ther manual action.
The entire project is structured as a monorepo and managed using Turborepo to streamôÙ
line development and dependency management [98]. One key operational constraint of
the PoC is that inference requests to the AI model are processed sequentially, one at a
time. To manage the finite VRAM available in the local GPU environment, this sequenôÙ
tial processing was implemented deliberately. Attempting to process multiple requests in
parallel would risk exceeding memory limits and causing inference failures.
It is important to note that this limitation is imposed by the hardware, not the software archiôÙ
tecture. The FastAPI backend is inherently capable of handling parallel requests, meanôÙ
ing the system is designed for concurrency. Therefore, the onôÙpremise setup proved ideal
for development and validation, and a proposed migration path to a scalable enterprise
solution that enables true concurrency will be detailed in Section 7.2.
6.2
Expert-in-the-Loop Workflow for Deviation Detection
The technical architecture of ALDEVI is built around a clear operational philosophy that
positions it as an expertôÙinôÙtheôÙloop system [99]. Its purpose is not to replace the critôÙ
ical judgment of Alfa Laval engineers, but to augment it by automating the most timeôÙ
consuming tasks. All findings are presented with full traceability, so the human expert
always retains final authority over every decision. The resulting workflow, visualized in
Figure 6.2, is organized into three distinct stages.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
66
==PAGE:81==
Stage 1
Establishing 
Ground Truth
Stage 2
Defining
Company 
Truths
Stage 3
Analyzing 
Deviations
Figure 6.2: The threeôÙstage expertôÙinôÙtheôÙloop workflow of ALDEVI.
The following subsections provide a detailed breakdown of each stage, describing the
systemãs capabilities and the interaction between the user and the AI.
6.2.1
Stage 1: Establishing the Ground Truth
Before any analysis can occur, the system must first be ãtaughtã what Alfa Laval considers
its standard practices. This foundational stage focuses on building a centralized, searchôÙ
able repository of all official company standards and guidelines, which will serve as the
ãground truthã for all subsequent analysis. An expert user navigates to the ãDocumentaôÙ
tionã section of the app, where they can upload internal documents, such as corporate
standards, product manuals, and technical guidelines.
Upon upload, ALDEVI initiates its ingestion pipeline in the background. Each document is
parsed, segmented into semantically coherent chunks using the RecursiveChunker stratôÙ
egy (800/400), and then vectorized. The resulting vectors are indexed and stored in a
dedicated alôÙstandards collection within the Qdrant database. The outcome of this stage
is a comprehensive and instantly searchable knowledge base.
6.2.2
Stage 2: Defining Company Truths
This stage transforms the tacit knowledge of experts and the explicit text of documents
into discrete, verifiable rules that the AI will use for its analysis. The process begins in the
ãTruthsã management interface, where an expert author writes a new CT. It involves two
key inputs:
1. A Company Truth, for example, ãAlfa Lavalãs standard coating is a twoôÙcoat systemã
(following the guidelines mentioned in Section 3.5).
2. A criticality score, included to support a future enhancement for prioritizing analôÙ
ysis results, although this ranking logic is not implemented in the current PoC (as
discussed further in Section 7.1).
Once the user saves the new CT, the system initiates a crucial, semiôÙautomated verifiôÙ
cation process. ALDEVI takes the statement as a query and searches the alôÙstandards
collection built in the previous stage. It then attempts to find the supporting evidence,
presenting the user with its best guess for the source document, page number, and an
excerpt of the relevant text.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
67
==PAGE:82==
The expert then performs the final, authoritative action by reviewing the AIãs proposed evôÙ
idence. If the systemãs suggestion is correct, they click ãVerify,ã whereas if it is incorrect,
the expert can manually correct the fields and save the new information. In doing so, they
create a permanent and auditable connection between the CT and its precise source.
Verification extends beyond internal data integrity, as it plays a vital role in business operôÙ
ations. It ensures that when a deviation is flagged, the engineer gains immediate access
to the exact internal standard that justifies the nonôÙconformance, which provides an unôÙ
deniable piece of evidence for negotiations with the customer. Only explicitly verified CTs
are activated for project analysis, and as a result, each rule is grounded in documented
and defensible facts.
6.2.3
Stage 3: Analyzing Deviations
While the automated deviation analysis in this final stage represents the primary technical
objective of this thesis, its success as a viable business solution is entirely dependent on
the robust foundation established in the preceding stages. A trustworthy analysis requires
a trustworthy knowledge base, making the expertôÙled processes of establishing ground
truth and verifying CTs indispensable prerequisites.
Building on this foundation, the core stage begins when an engineer creates a new project
within ALDEVI and uploads all relevant customer documents. As a result, the analysis
is automatically initiated, with the system iterating through every single verified CT and
executing the full Hybrid Retrieval pipeline against the customer documents.
As results are generated, they populate an interactive table designed for efficient, evidenceôÙ
based review. Each row is dedicated to a single CT, presenting the CT statement, its veriôÙ
fied internal evidence, the relevant snippet from the customerãs documents, and a precise
citation with document name and page number. The final columns display the AIãs outôÙ
put: a concise explanation for its reasoning, followed by its suggested colorôÙcoded label
(Deviation, No Deviation, or Unclear). This dense, scannable layout provides engineers
with all the critical information needed for rapid decisionôÙmaking and ready for export.
The interface enables engineers to validate the AIãs findings efficiently. A suggestion can
be accepted with a single click or overridden with a brief justification. Every decision is
logged, creating a complete audit trail and simultaneously generating a rich dataset of
expertôÙvalidated examples. This feedback loop, which captures both confirmations and
corrections, provides an invaluable foundation for future system enhancements, such as
continuous model fineôÙtuning and more sophisticated prompt engineering, as discussed
further in Section 7.1.
The expertôÙcentric workflow emphasizes traceability and user authority. By automating
the information search and backing each AI suggestion with citable evidence, it reduces
manual review to a focused, minutesôÙlong validation task. It directly targets the challenges
of inconsistency and lost knowledge inherent in the original operational bottleneck by
keeping the engineer in complete control, supported by transparent, AIôÙdriven insights.
Having detailed the design and intended workflow of the ALDEVI prototype, the research
now turns to empirical validation. The following sections will present the evidence of the
systemãs realôÙworld performance, first through a qualitative study with its intended expert
users, followed by a rigorous quantitative test.
6.3
Qualitative Validation
After implementing the functional prototype, the next critical step involved validating its
design, workflow, and perceived value with the intended endôÙusers. To achieve this, a
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
68
==PAGE:83==
qualitative study was conducted that included a presentation, a live demonstration, and a
followôÙup survey.
6.3.1
Methodology
On May 27th, 2025, the ALDEVI prototype was presented to a group of approximately 25
engineers from the E&S department in Sû¡borg. The audience included domain experts
responsible for contract analysis and deviation detection. The 25ôÙminute session featured
a presentation covering the projectãs background and core concepts, a live demonstration
of the prototypeãs key features, and a brief Q&A.
To capture structured feedback, a voluntary survey was distributed to all attendees followôÙ
ing the session. The survey was designed to gauge the expertsã reception of the project
and their perception of its potential impact, using a combination of Likert scale ratings,
multipleôÙchoice questions, and an openôÙended question for suggestions. The complete
survey instrument is available in Appendix J.
A total of seven responses were received. It is crucial to note that, although this sample
size is insufficient to draw statistically generalizable conclusions about the entire departôÙ
ment, the detailed feedback from these domain experts provides invaluable qualitative
insights into the prototypeãs reception and identifies key considerations for its future deôÙ
velopment.
6.3.2
Results and Analysis
The feedback gathered from the domain experts was overwhelmingly positive, providing
strong qualitative validation for the projectãs direction and the prototypeãs design. The full
survey results, including the figures discussed in this section and the complete responses,
are also available in Appendix J.
As shown in the LikertôÙscale data in Appendix Figure J.1, all seven respondents (100%)
agreed or strongly agreed that the problem statement was relevant, the core concepts
were clear, and that ALDEVI could meaningfully improve their workflow. Therefore, the
findings confirm a strong alignment between the prototype and the usersã needs.
When asked about specific impacts, the expertsã responses highlighted two key themes.
First, their focus was squarely on the core value proposition: ãFind potential deviations
automaticallyã (43%) and ãReduce time per projectã (29%) were the most valued features.
Second, their estimates for time savings varied widely, a point clarified by a key piece
of openôÙended feedback: ãWe need to make sure it is trustworthy so we completely can
remove the manual reading of bid documents.ã This insight reveals that while experts are
optimistic, they identify that the toolãs ultimate impact is contingent on its proven reliability.
In summary, the qualitative study indicates that ALDEVI is perceived as a highly relevant
and valuable solution by its target users. However, their feedback also underscores that
this perceived value must be backed by objective performance. The research now turns
from perception to proof by subjecting the prototype to a rigorous quantitative field test.

time per CT consistently between 13.6 and 14.2 seconds. As expected, Search Time
scaled with project size: Project Y (618 chunks) was fastest, while Project Z (2,566
chunks) was slowest. In contrast, Inference Time remained stable across all projects,
averaging 359 seconds. This consistency reflects the fixed number of retrieved chunks,
which imposes a uniform workload on the generative model. Overall, the system delivers
predictable, fast analysis, even for large and complex projects.
Final Accuracy Metrics
With the systemãs efficiency established, the final and most critical test focuses on meaôÙ
suring its accuracy in detecting deviations. To evaluate the system, a domain expert
assessed each prediction made by ALDEVI. According to the exact expertôÙinôÙtheôÙloop
workflow detailed in Section 6.2.3, the expert relied on the evidence and citations proôÙ
vided by the system as a starting point to perform the final crossôÙreference and determine
whether a deviation truly existed. The manual validation step was timed and required
approximately 4 minutes per item.
The expertãs final verdict for each case, indicating whether the AIãs initial prediction was
correct or incorrect, served as the ground truth for scoring. Subsequently, the systemãs
performance was calculated by comparing its initial predictions to these expertôÙvalidated
outcomes. The final metrics appear in Table 6.6.

The results confirm that the system is highly effective at its primary objective: identifying
the vast majority of true deviations. As detailed in Table 6.6, the system achieved a
remarkably high and stable macro average recall of 88.4%, successfully identifying 31
out of 35 total deviations across all projects. This performance underscores the Hybrid
architectureãs strength in minimizing the critical business risk of false negatives.
Overall, the system achieved a strong macro average F1 score of 0.733 and an accuracy
of 0.753, indicating robust and reliable performance. However, the results also reveal
that performance varies with project complexity, a key insight into the systemãs realôÙworld
behavior. For instance, Project Z, a large but wellôÙstructured project, yielded an excellent
F1 score of 0.89. In contrast, Project Y proved more challenging, resulting in a significantly
higher FPR (47.4%) and a consequently lower F1 score of 0.58. This variance is likely
attributable to the nature of the source documents, which have fewer pages and chunks
and may contain more ambiguity, making it harder for the retrieval system to identify a
clear signal.
Despite this variability, these accuracy results represent a significant step towards solving
the operational bottleneck described in Section 1.2. While a fully manual review can take
upwards of 30 hours, the AIôÙassisted workflow demonstrated significant efficiency gains.
In this experiment, validating the 27 test truths required approximately 2 hours of expert
effort per project. Extrapolating this to a more comprehensive, realôÙworld ruleset of 200
CT would result in an estimated validation time of around 13 hours. By automating the
timeôÙconsuming search and comparison tasks, which the system performs in minutes,
ALDEVI fundamentally transforms the engineerãs role from a manual searcher to an effiôÙ
cient, focused validator. The full strategic and financial implications of this efficiency gain
will be analyzed in detail in Section 7.3.
6.5
Augmented Capabilities
Beyond the core deviation detection workflow, the ALDEVI prototype was further extended
with features to enhance engineer productivity. The most significant is a conversational
chat interface, a capability efficiently implemented by leveraging the existing Hybrid RAG
pipeline. By simply swapping the detection Signature for a questionôÙanswering one, the
system demonstrates the modularity of its architecture. This chat functionality enables
engineers to query the internal knowledge base to clarify company standards (e.g., ãWhat
is our standard warranty period?ã) or examine customer documents to locate specific,
nonôÙdeviating details critical to the projectãs scope (e.g., ãDoes the customer require any
specific certifications for the motors?ã).
Additionally, the prototype includes functionality to export the final, expertôÙverified deviôÙ
ation report into an Excel format, streamlining the sharing of findings with stakeholders.
It is important to note that these augmented capabilities, while demonstrating the platôÙ
formãs potential as a comprehensive analysis hub, were not part of the formal quantitative
validation detailed in the previous sections.
In summary, this chapter has detailed the implementation of the ALDEVI prototype and
presented empirical evidence (both qualitative and quantitative) that validates its ability to
accurately detect deviations and enhance the engineering workflow. With the prototypeãs
performance and user acceptance demonstrated, all requirements for Gate 4 ã Prototype
Validation have been fulfilled. The research now proceeds to the final chapter, which will
synthesize the findings, assess their strategic and financial impact for Alfa Laval, and
provide conclusions on the projectãs contributions and future potential.

7
Discussion, Impact, and Conclusion
7.1
Discussion
The preceding chapters have detailed the systematic engineering and validation of the
ALDEVI prototype. This section now synthesizes these findings to provide comprehenôÙ
sive answers to the research questions posed in Section 1.3. By linking the experimental
results to the three investigative pillars, this discussion highlights the thesisãs main contriôÙ
butions and sets the stage for the final analysis of the systemãs impact.
7.1.1
The Knowledge Foundation
The first investigative pillar asked: ãHow can unstructured company knowledge be effecôÙ
tively structured to serve as a reliable foundation for an AIôÙdriven system?ã. This research
demonstrates that for a specialized industrial domain, the answer lies not in rigid, offôÙtheôÙ
shelf standards, but in a flexible framework capable of capturing both formal policies
and tacit expert knowledge.
The primary evidence for this conclusion is the headôÙtoôÙhead comparison presented in
Section 5.2. An initial system built on a formal, ISOôÙbased requirements framework, which
achieved high recall (81.8%), was rendered impractical due to an extremely high FPR
(74.9%) that would overwhelm engineers with false alarms, resulting in a poor F1 score
of 0.51. In direct contrast, the customôÙengineered Company Truth framework delivered a
balanced and robust performance, achieving a strong F1 score of 0.801 with a low FPR of
just 23.3%. This empirical result confirms that standard requirement formalisms are too
rigid for this domain. They cannot naturally represent critical business rules, such as negôÙ
ative constraints (e.g., ãWe do not offer X beyond five yearsã), or contextual commercial
logic.
The success of the CT Framework is further validated by its practical application at scale.
The dual knowledge mining pipeline, detailed in Section 4.3, systematically processed
thousands of ambiguous expert comments and formal documents, successfully engineerôÙ
ing a canonical knowledge base of 2,532 CTs. This demonstrates that the framework is
not only theoretically superior in a controlled test but also robust and expressive enough
to handle the complexities of a realôÙworld industrial environment.
7.1.2
The System Optimization
The second investigative pillar asked: ãWhich RAG architecture and components provide
the most accurate and efficient performance for deviation detection in this specialized
industrial domain?ã. This research provides a definitive answer: a Hybrid Retrieval arôÙ
chitecture, integrating sparse, dense, and lateôÙinteraction methods, delivers the optimal
balance of performance and efficiency. The findings consistently show that for this task,
the quality of the retrieval pipeline is a more critical factor for success than the raw power
of the generative model.
A twoôÙpart experimental process supports this conclusion. First, a systematic, componentôÙ
level optimization in Chapter 4 established a toolkit of highôÙquality components, including
the efficient Phi-4 generator and the domainôÙtuned AL-MPNet embedding model. SecôÙ
ond, the systemôÙlevel evaluation in Chapter 5 provided the most decisive evidence. Using
a custom twoôÙstage protocol to handle the complex evaluation data, the Hybrid Retrieval
architecture outperformed all other textôÙbased systems, achieving the highest F1 score
(0.814) with a practical latency of just 12.2 seconds.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
75
==PAGE:90==
In contrast, alternative strategies proved less effective. Architectures focused solely on
the generator, such as imposing a CoT reasoning process, suffered a catastrophic drop
in recall to 39.9%, suggesting that excessive reasoning complexity can be fragile. SimiôÙ
larly, while multimodal systems achieved the highest recall (up to 88.5%), this sensitivity
came at the cost of an unacceptably high FPR (up to 53.4%) and prohibitive latency (41
seconds). These results lead to a key insight: under the hardware and quantization conôÙ
straints of this PoC, investing in retrieval quality yields more reliable outcomes than simply
increasing model size or complexity. The more efficient Phi-4 model, when paired with
a superior retrieval system, delivered the best overall performance.
7.1.3
The Validation Framework
The final investigative pillar asked: ãWhat is a practical methodology for validating the sysôÙ
temãs performance and quantifying its impact on the realôÙworld engineering workflow?ã.
This research addresses the issue by proposing and executing a twoôÙstage validation
framework that combines qualitative user acceptance with rigorous quantitative perforôÙ
mance measurement.
The first stage, qualitative validation (Section 6.3), established the systemãs relevance
and perceived value. Through a live demonstration and survey, domain experts confirmed
that the ALDEVI prototype addressed a critical business need, providing the necessary
justification to proceed with objective testing.
The second stage was a quantitative field test (Section 6.4) designed to mirror a realôÙworld
production scenario. This test employed a oneôÙtoôÙone evaluation protocol, where each of
the 27 test CTs produced a single, actionable output for review. An ãexpertôÙinôÙtheôÙloopã
scoring method was used, where the expertãs final verdict on each prediction served as
the ground truth. This approach provided a practical and unbiased means of measuring
the systemãs performance on three novel projects.
By integrating both userôÙcentric feedback and objective metrics, this framework provides
a comprehensive answer to the research question. A practical methodology must first
confirm user relevance before quantifying performance. The final results of this process
(a macro average recall of 88.4% and an F1 score of 0.733) provide clear, dataôÙdriven
evidence of the systemãs success, validating the framework itself as a practical approach
for evaluating complex industrial RAG systems.
7.1.4
The Primary Research Question
Ultimately, the findings from each investigative pillar converge to provide a comprehenôÙ
sive answer to the central research question of the thesis: ãHow can a RAG system be
engineered to transform Alfa Lavalãs manual review of customer tender documents by
accurately detecting deviations and increasing operational efficiency?ã.
The research demonstrates that such a system can be successfully engineered by sysôÙ
tematically addressing each of its pillars. A robust Knowledge Foundation was estabôÙ
lished by creating the flexible CT Framework, which proved empirically superior to rigid,
standard approaches. Rigorous System Optimization identified a Hybrid Retrieval archiôÙ
tecture as the optimal design, confirming that retrieval quality is the most critical lever for
performance in this domain. Finally, a practical Validation Framework was developed
and applied, culminating in a field test where the resulting ALDEVI prototype demonôÙ
strated its realôÙworld effectiveness. The systemãs proven ability to reliably identify nonôÙ
conformities and automate the manual workflow directly answers the research question,
providing both accurate deviation detection and a significant increase in operational effiôÙ
ciency.

7.2
Path to Production
The successful validation of the ALDEVI prototype confirms its technical feasibility. HowôÙ
ever, its onôÙpremise design is not wellôÙsuited for enterpriseôÙwide use. As a result, miôÙ
grating the system to a scalable cloud architecture within Alfa Lavalãs existing Microsoft
Azure ecosystem becomes the logical next step for production. By making this transition,
the system will benefit from enhanced performance, improved availability, and removal
of the sequential inference bottleneck imposed by local hardware constraints. The proôÙ
posed production architecture, illustrated in Figure 7.1, substitutes the prototypeãs local
components with their enterpriseôÙgrade Azure counterparts.

The two primary upgrades are:
1. Database: The local SQLite database is replaced by a managed Azure SQL Database

2. Inference engine: The selfôÙhosted Llama.cpp server is swapped for the Azure OpeôÙ

no longer a need for local GPU management, and data sovereignty is maintained.
A key strength of the prototypeãs design is that this migration is lowôÙrisk and straightforôÙ
ward. The application logic was intentionally decoupled from the infrastructure. Thanks
to the use of an ObjectôÙRelational Mapper (ORM), switching the database requires only a
configuration change. Similarly, the DSPy framework allows the LLM backend to be rediôÙ
rected from the local server to the Azure API with minimal effort, leaving the core prompt
programs untouched.

section translates the technical achievements into concrete business terms, quantifying
their financial and operational implications. To provide a complete view, the analysis is
organized in three parts:
1. Strategic Value: First, we assess the operational impact by quantifying efficiency

and per project to assess efficiency.

speed) and a set of operational assumptions, primarily a focused ruleset of 200 CTs.

While these results highlight the significant operational and financial benefits of the proôÙ

and balanced perspective, the following section addresses the systemãs key limitations.
7.4
Limitations
To provide a dataôÙdriven assessment of the systemãs limitations, a series of experiments
were conducted to quantify the impact of key risks and their potential mitigations. Each
experiment, benchmarked against the Hybrid Retrieval baseline, was designed to isolate
and test a specific risk identified during the development process. The performance of
these experimental variants against the baseline is summarized in Table 7.5. The risks
investigated include:
ãÂ Knowledge Base Coverage: The systemãs effectiveness is fundamentally limited
by the completeness of its CT knowledge base. If a rule is not captured, any related
deviation will go undetected. This risk was assessed qualitatively, as a quantitative
test was impractical.
ãÂ Model Quality: This risk concerns the base LLMãs inherent knowledge and its reôÙ
silience to quantization. A direct comparison with larger, proprietary models was
outside the scope and compliance constraints of this PoC, but its potential impact
is discussed qualitatively.
ãÂ Data Quality: The systemãs performance is highly dependent on the clarity of the
humanôÙauthored CTs. To test the impact of ambiguity, a variant was created where
the original, validated truths were programmatically altered to be less precise, simôÙ
ulating the risk of poorly phrased inputs.
ãÂ Truth Prioritization: The baseline system treats all truths equally. To understand
the risk of this approach, a variant simulated a simple, unrefined prioritization stratôÙ
egy in which truths linked to formal standards (e.g., ATEX) were weighted more
heavily, testing whether this naive approach could skew the results.
ãÂ Retrieval Quality: The quality of the initial document parsing is a critical but often
overlooked variable. To measure its impact, a variant was created that replaced the
baseline PyMuPDF library with the more robust Docling library, testing the mitigation
of using a superior parser.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
81
==PAGE:96==
ãÂ ZeroôÙShot Prompting: The baselineãs reliance on zeroôÙshot prompting can lead to
hallucinations. To mitigate this, a variant was enhanced with KNN FewôÙShot promptôÙ
ing, which dynamically injects three relevant, previously validated examples into the
prompt for each new query.
ãÂ Taxonomy: The semantic classification of CTs served a critical organizational purôÙ
pose but was not functionally leveraged to drive system logic, such as prioritizing
results. This limitation was assessed qualitatively based on its potential impact on
workflow efficiency.
ãÂ Output Traceability: This risk, related to incorrect source citations eroding user
trust, was assessed qualitatively. A quantitative test was impractical as it would
require a manual, pageôÙbyôÙpage review of every generated response.
ãÂ Label Instability: To test a mitigation for minor label inconsistencies from the LLM,
a variant was created that used a majorityôÙvote (3x) system, calling the model three
times for the same query and choosing the most common label.

7.4.1
Interpretation of Findings
Beyond the numbers in Table 7.5, the qualitatively assessed risks complete the picture of
system performance. The analysis below synthesizes these quantitative and qualitative
insights for every risk category.
Knowledge Base Coverage: The most significant limitation is the completeness of the
operational knowledge base, as the system is only as good as its rules. The research
generated over 2,500 truths, yet its practical scope was limited to much smaller sets: the
final validation used only 27, while business projections relied on 200. Any rule outside
this active set is a blind spot that guarantees false negatives. This makes knowledge gaps
a highôÙimpact, highôÙlikelihood risk, whose solution is more organizational than technical,
requiring a sustained effort in curation.
Model Quality: A fundamental limitation is the base LLMãs inherent knowledge, a risk
amplified by quantization. The model may lack the specialized understanding to equate
different standards (e.g., ISO 600034ôÙ1 and IEEE 112). More critically, a model with an
outdated knowledge cutoff cannot correctly reason about novel concepts introduced in
new standards, even when the text is retrieved by the RAG system.
Data Quality: The experiment confirms that data quality is the most critical factor for reliôÙ
able performance. When the dataset included artificially ambiguous CTs, recall dropped
dramatically to 0.622, resulting in an F1 score of only 0.722. In practical terms, the model
failed to identify a significant number of actual deviations. These results demonstrate that
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
82
==PAGE:97==
biased or poorôÙquality data can create the illusion of firm performance on specific metrics,
ultimately leading to an unreliable system.
Truth Prioritization: Applying a naive, criticalityôÙweighted decision strategy caused sigôÙ
nificant harm. Although recall matched the baseline at 0.797, the FPR increased from
23.3% to 78.6%. As a result, precision collapsed, and the F1 score dropped to just 0.6.
These findings underscore that while weighted decision strategies may offer potential benôÙ
efits, their effectiveness depends on careful design and thorough evaluation by domain
experts.
Retrieval Quality: Selecting an appropriate document parser serves as a key lever for
improving performance, as demonstrated by the experiment comparing Docling and PyôÙ
MuPDF. While both parsers achieved identical recall, Doclingãs superior handling of comôÙ
plex layouts reduced false positives, boosting the F1 score from 0.814 to 0.843. This
finding provides the empirical justification for the decision to upgrade to Docling for the
final validation in Chapter 6. It underscores that frontôÙend parsing quality has a direct
influence on system performance and should be a primary consideration in any deployôÙ
ment.
ZeroôÙShot Prompting: Introducing inôÙcontext examples provides a lowôÙcost yet highly
effective mitigation strategy. By dynamically injecting three relevant examples into the
prompt using KNN FewôÙShot, recall improved from 0.797 to 0.851. This adjustment also
boosted the F1 score from 0.814 to 0.834. The inclusion of concrete examples helps the
model better distinguish between ãDeviationã and ãNo Deviationã in ambiguous scenarios.
Moreover, this approach reduces the risk of hallucination and does not require expensive
fineôÙtuning.
Taxonomy: The prototypeãs semantic taxonomy was critical for organizing the 2,500+
CTs but did not drive the systemãs logic. While this prevented features like intelligent routôÙ
ing and riskôÙbased prioritization, the impact on the prototypeãs core function is considered
low, as the system still achieved its primary goal of highôÙrecall deviation detection. Indeed,
a more practical enterprise approach identified during the research is to classify truths by
departmental ownership (e.g., Sales, R&D, Product Management) to enable these more
advanced, efficiencyôÙdriving workflows.
Output Traceability: Although not directly measured, the risk of incorrect citations reôÙ
mains a notable qualitative limitation. Each error, even if easy for an expert to correct,
diminishes user trust and increases manual verification time.
Label Instability: Implementing a majorityôÙvote system to enhance label stability turned
out to be an unfavorable tradeôÙoff. The method aimed to reduce inconsistency. However,
the F1 score dropped from 0.814 to 0.756, and computation time tripled. Although label
instability remains a limitation, these results suggest that majorityôÙvote thresholding is not
a practical solution, as the costs in terms of performance and efficiency outweigh the
potential benefits.
7.4.2
Risk Assessment
Synthesizing these experimental findings allows us to position each risk in a likelihoodôÙ
impact matrix, providing a clear, dataôÙdriven, and prioritized overview. This assessment,
as shown in Figure 7.2, concludes that Knowledge Base Coverage, Model Quality, and
Data Quality are the highestôÙpriority risks due to their high potential impact. Conversely,
risks such as Label Instability are deemed low priority, as the experiments showed their
potential impact to be minor and their mitigation strategies to be prohibitively costly.

Ultimately, this dataôÙdriven analysis not only outlines potential weaknesses but also highôÙ

clusions to be drawn. It sets a clear, evidenceôÙbased agenda for future research, as
discussed in the next section.
7.5
Conclusions and Future Work
7.5.1
Conclusions
Manual contract review at Alfa Laval represents a critical operational bottleneck, consumôÙ
ing over 30 engineer hours per project and introducing significant compliance risk. This
thesis successfully addressed this challenge by engineering and validating ALDEVI, an
endôÙtoôÙend RAG solution that automates deviation detection. The systemãs success was
built on a sequence of core achievements: the novel Company Truth Framework was
first engineered to create a viable knowledge foundation, which in turn enabled the optiôÙ
mization of a Hybrid Retrieval architecture that outperformed eight alternatives. Finally, a
twoôÙstage validation on novel, realôÙworld projects confirmed the prototypeãs ability to mitôÙ
igate this risk, demonstrating an 88.4% recall rate and transforming the 30+ hour manual
review into a focused, AIôÙassisted workflow. However, this performance is fundamentally
dependent on the quality and completeness of the underlying knowledge base, as the
system can only identify deviations for which a CT exists.
The research yielded several key insights into engineering applied AI systems. ForeôÙ
most among them is that under the hardware and quantization constraints of this PoC,
the quality of the retrieval pipeline proved more decisive for success than the raw power
of the generative model. Secondly, the work underscores that generic formalisms are inôÙ
sufficient for specialized domains. A flexible, domainôÙspecific knowledge representation
is indispensable for capturing the nuance of expert work. Finally, this thesis serves as
a cautionary tale against architectural complexity. Both intricate reasoning patterns, like
CoT, and nascent multimodal systems proved less robust than a wellôÙoptimized, textôÙonly
pipeline. This demonstrates that for applied systems, robust foundational engineering is
more critical than pursuing advanced features that are compromised by realôÙworld conôÙ
straints like quantization.
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
84
==PAGE:99==
Ultimately, this thesis delivers two primary contributions. For Alfa Laval, it provides a
practical, dataôÙvalidated blueprint for modernizing a missionôÙcritical workflow, relevant not
only to the pilot business unit but also to other Alfa Laval divisions and to companies that
routinely review customer tender and specification documents, offering a clear path to
reclaiming over 1400 engineering hours annually and increasing project throughput by
nearly fourfold. For the applied AI community, it contributes a comprehensive case study
of engineering and validating a RAG system in a highôÙstakes industrial setting, offering
realôÙworld insights to complement theoretical research. It demonstrates that the true value
of applied AI lies not in replacing human experts but in amplifying their judgment, bridging
the gap between AIôÙdriven analysis and human intuition.
The ultimate validation of this research is the formal recognition from Alfa Laval. As deôÙ
tailed in the supervisorãs endorsement (Appendix L), the companyãs commitment to move
the project into a production phase confirms that ALDEVI successfully addressed a critical
business challenge.
7.5.2
Future Work
Short-Term: Enhancing the Core System
The most immediate priorities involve addressing the highestôÙimpact risks to enhance the
core systemãs robustness and accuracy. The first step is to execute the ãPath to ProducôÙ
tionã (Section 7.2) by migrating the system to Azure, which provides compliant access
to larger, more powerful models and directly mitigates the risks associated with model
quality and quantization. Alongside improving the model, ensuring the quality of its inôÙ
put is paramount. Therefore, a formal process for authoring CTs should be established,
including a style guide and training for domain experts. Finally, to create a system that
improves over time, a continuous learning loop should be implemented. By capturing exôÙ
pert corrections, the system can use this growing dataset of labeled examples to enhance
its prompting (KNN FewôÙShot) and dynamically refine its models periodically.
Mid-Term: Expanding the Functional Scope
Beyond enhancing the current system, future work should aim to extend its capabilities
to tackle related business challenges. A key area for further exploration is the handling
of clarifications, particularly when customer requirements are ambiguous or incomplete.
Since this topic was outside the scope of the current thesis, a helpful first step could be to
analyze the systemãs ãUnclearã outputs to better distinguish between actual clarification
needs and simple gaps in information. Additionally, transitioning to a stronger cloud enviôÙ
ronment enables a reevaluation of multimodal and CoT architectures without the hardware
and quantization limitations of the current system.
Long-Term: Integrated AI Ecosystem
The longôÙterm goal is to evolve the system from a standalone tool into a live, integrated
AI ecosystem.
A key step is adopting the emerging Model Context Protocol (MCP),
which has been gaining traction as a way to enable realôÙtime access to authoritative data
sources. Integrating MCP into ALDEVI would allow dynamic retrieval of upôÙtoôÙdate inôÙ
formation from internal systems like SharePoint or Product Lifecycle Management (PLM)
tools, as well as external regulatory sources such as ISO databases. By avoiding static
inputs, the system could base its analysis on live, alwaysôÙupdated information.
Pursuing these enhancements will evolve ALDEVI into a robust tool designed to amplify
expert judgment. By automating the laborious task of search and comparison, it frees
human talent for the critical, highôÙstakes decisions that define their role. That integration
between AIôÙdriven analysis and human intuition is not merely a path to efficiency, but
the engine of competitive advantage in modern industrial engineering.

class ExtractCompanyTruths(dspy.Signature):
2
ïÑïÑ"""
3

a principled, process-driven approach.
,ã
4
5
ïÑ**Persona:ïÑ** You act as an Alfa Laval internal compliance expert.
6
7
ïÑ**Principles:ïÑ**
8
- A Company Truth (CT) is a succinct, precise, and verifiable
statement of internal practice, constraint, or decision that
impacts compliance.
,ã
,ã
9
- CTs must capture only Alfa Laval's internal standards or
reasoning, never external details or document references.
,ã
10
- Each CT is expressed as a standalone sentence, built from any of
the following blocks as needed:
,ã
11
[Who/What] [Action] [Subject] [Condition] [Purpose, optional]
[Performance criteria, optional]
,ã
12
- Do not infer or elaborateãextract only what is explicitly stated.
13

one sentence.
,ã
14

documentation.
,ã
15

reuse.
,ã
16
17

18

19

per idea).
,ã

and verifiable.
,ã
21

,ã

,ã
18

,ã

,ã
20
ïÑïÑ"""

,ã

G
Vision Prompts
Positive Description Prompt
1
Persona:
2
You are an expert data annotation assistant specialized in image
analysis for fine-tuning image embedding models.
,ã
3
4
Principles:
5
- Each description must accurately describe the image content.
6
- Descriptions should be approximately 25 words each.
7
- Each should vary naturally, mimicking how different users might
describe the same image.
,ã
8
- Output must be valid JSON, using only the "positives" key.
9
10
Process:
11
1. Analyze the provided image carefully.
12
2. Generate 5 positive descriptions that each capture the image
faithfully, differing in language or focus as a user might do.
,ã
13
3. Output the results as plain text JSON in the format:
14
{
15
"positives": ["<Description 1>", "<Description 2>", "<Description
3>", "<Description 4>", "<Description 5>"]
,ã
16
}
Listing G.1: Prompt for generating positive image descriptions.
Negative Description Prompt
1
Persona:
2
You are an expert data annotation assistant specialized in image
analysis for fine-tuning image embedding models.
,ã
3
4
Principles:
5
- Each description must be approximately 25 words.
6
- Descriptions must be completely unrelated to the provided image.
7
- Output must be valid JSON, using only the "negatives" key.
8
9
Process:
10
1. Ignore the content of the image when generating descriptions.
11
2. Generate 5 negative descriptions, each describing something that
does not appear in the image.
,ã
12
3. Output the results as plain text JSON in the format:
13
{
14
"negatives": ["<Negative Description 1>", "<Negative Description
2>", "<Negative Description 3>", "<Negative Description 4>",
"<Negative Description 5>"]
,ã
,ã
15
}
Listing G.2: Prompt for generating negative image descriptions.

Recommendation and Impact Statement: 
ALDEVI Thesis Project at Alfa Laval 
By Hans Christian Schubell, AI & Data Solutions Manager, Business Unit Decanters 
Date: July 18, 2025 
1. Introduction and Personal Endorsement 
As the main company contact for this thesis project, I have worked closely with Diogo Miguel 
Lopes Carvalho throughout the development of the ALDEVI system (Alfa Laval Deviation Insight). 
It has been a pleasure to support this collaboration, which I believe stands as a strong example 
of how academic research can lead to meaningful innovation in industry. 
From the outset, Diogo demonstrated a structured, curious, and business-oriented mindset. His 
ability to understand our engineering processes, identify pain points, and methodically explore 
how AI could address them was genuinely impressive. This project was not just a technical 
exercise; it was a clear attempt to solve a real and pressing business problem. 
2. Business Context and Problem Relevance 
At Alfa Laval customer-specific projects are central to our operations. However, each project 
carries unique complexity, often resulting in risks that are difficult to detect during the early 
stages, particularly when evaluating customer tender documents and aligning them with internal 
standards. 
One of the key risks we face is entering into contracts that later reveal difficult or costly delivery 
obligations. In our experience, a single problematic project can wipe out the profit of multiple 
successful ones. Mitigating such risk is not just desirable, it is essential. 
The ALDEVI system directly addresses this by supporting the analysis of tender documents with 
AI. It introduces a structured way to flag potential issues early, helping us make better-informed 
decisions before commitments are made. 
3. Value Across Efficiency, Risk, and Knowledge Management 
Risk Mitigation 
ALDEVI helps us systematically assess customer requirements against what we know we can 
confidently deliver. This makes it easier to spot potential contractual pitfalls early, reducing the 
chance of costly surprises later in the project lifecycle. 
L
Statement from Alfa Laval
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
118
==PAGE:133==
 
Operational Efficiency 
While automation often implies time savings, what matters more in our case is whose time we 
save. The engineers involved in document validation are highly specialized experts. Freeing up 
their capacity means we can accelerate project delivery without compromising quality. 
Knowledge Repeatability 
One of the most exciting outcomes of this project is the foundation it lays for building a reusable 
knowledge base. Instead of treating each project as a standalone case, ALDEVI allows us to 
retain and apply previous insights, enabling smarter decisions across future projects. 
4. Broader Organizational Relevance 
Although the project was focused on the Decanters business unit, the underlying problem of 
validating complex customer requirements is universal across our Engineering and Design 
departments. That makes this solution highly relevant not just for Alfa Laval but for the broader 
industrial sector. 
There is also future potential for ALDEVI to evolve into a chat-based copilot, helping engineers 
navigate internal project data and documentation. While this was not part of the current thesis 
scope, it has already sparked interest from teams within Engineering and Supply. 
5. Project Execution and Collaboration 
Throughout the project, Diogo showed strong initiative and discipline. His technical choices were 
grounded in practical constraints and business relevance. He did not aim to build the most 
complex system, but rather the most useful one, something that could genuinely be adopted and 
scaled within our environment. 
He approached challenges not just with technical skill, but with a strong understanding of how 
people work and how decisions are made in a real industrial setting. That mindset is rare and 
highly valuable. 
6. Company Commitment and Next Steps 
We are very happy with the initial results and have decided to move forward with the next 
implementation phase, which includes: 
ãÂ Development of a production-grade system 
ãÂ Integration with enterprise tools and workflows 
ãÂ Pilot expansion to other departments 
ãÂ Long-term adoption planning 
Compliance Through AI: Contract Analysis with LLMs and RAG
119
==PAGE:134==
 
The initial success of this project has not only validated the idea but also energized internal 
stakeholders. 
7. Final Thoughts 
In summary, I fully recommend this thesis project as a example of impactful, applied AI 
research. Diogo has not only delivered a technically sound proof of concept but also created real 
business value and opened the door to broader innovation in our organization. 
It has been a privilege to support this work. I look forward to following how ALDEVI evolves and 
contributes to Alfa Lavalãs project delivery. 
 
 
 
 
Hans Christian Schubell

Alfa Laval
